y_P = k/2 - ((h + r1)/k)*(x_P - (h - r1)/2). As we did.

Now compute (h + r1) and (d + r2 - h). There might be relation between (h + r1) and something like (d + r2 - h) after simplification using the expressions for h. Let's compute h explicitly.

h = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d).

Then compute (d + r2 - h). Substitute h:

d + r2 - h = d + r2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/(2d) = (2d^2 + 2d r2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)) / (2d) = (2d^2 + 2d r2 - d^2 - r1^2 + r2^2) / (2d) = (d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2) / (2d).

That's not obviously simplifying with (h + r1). Compute (h + r1):

h + r1 = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/(2d) + r1 = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2 + 2d r1) / (2d) = (d^2 + 2d r1 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d). So that's ( (d+r1)^2 - r2^2 )/(2d). Similarly, d + r2 - h = (d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2) / (2d). That is something like ( (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 )/(2d)? Let's check: (d + r2)^2 = d^2 + 2d r2 + r2^2. Then (d+r2)^2 - r1^2 = d^2 + 2d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2. That's exactly the numerator we have. So indeed:

d + r2 - h = ((d + r2)^2 - r1^2) / (2d).

Thus we have:

(h + r1) = ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d).
(d + r2 - h) = ((d + r2)^2 - r1^2) / (2d).

Now note that (h + r1) and (d + r2 - h) are both positive? Possibly. But anyway.

Now compute (h + r1)(d + r2 - h) = [ ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) * ((d + r2)^2 - r1^2) ] / (4d^2). That looks messy. However, we might also compute y_P in a more clever way: Since P lies on perpendicular bisector of CD, and also on perpendicular bisector of AC. Perhaps we can compute P by solving the system of equations for the circumcenter of ACD directly using coordinates. Let's denote P = (x, y). Then distances:

|P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2 = |P - D|^2.

So we have:

(1) (x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = (x + r1)^2 + y^2.   (since C = (-r1,0))
(2) (x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = (x - (d + r2))^2 + y^2.

From (1) expand:

(x^2 - 2hx + h^2) + (y^2 - 2ky + k^2) = (x^2 + 2r1 x + r1^2) + y^2.

Cancel x^2 + y^2:

-2hx + h^2 - 2ky + k^2 = 2r1 x + r1^2.

Bring terms:

-2hx - 2r1 x - 2ky + (h^2 + k^2 - r1^2) = 0.

But h^2 + k^2 = r1^2, so h^2 + k^2 - r1^2 = 0. So we have:

-2(h + r1) x - 2k y = 0 => divide -2: (h + r1) x + k y = 0.   (Eq A)

Similarly, from (2):

(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = (x - (d + r2))^2 + y^2.

Expand:

x^2 - 2hx + h^2 + y^2 - 2ky + k^2 = x^2 - 2(d + r2) x + (d + r2)^2 + y^2.

Cancel x^2 + y^2:

-2hx + h^2 - 2ky + k^2 = -2(d + r2) x + (d + r2)^2.

Bring terms:

-2hx + 2(d + r2) x - 2ky + (h^2 + k^2 - (d + r2)^2) = 0.

Now h^2 + k^2 = r1^2. So:

-2hx + 2(d + r2) x - 2ky + (r1^2 - (d + r2)^2) = 0.

Combine x terms: 2( (d + r2) - h) x - 2k y + (r1^2 - (d + r2)^2) = 0.

Divide 2: ((d + r2) - h) x - k y + (r1^2 - (d + r2)^2)/2 = 0.

So we have:

((d + r2) - h) x - k y + (r1^2 - (d + r2)^2)/2 = 0.   (Eq B)

Now we also have the perpendicular bisector of CD (vertical) is x = (d + r2 - r1)/2. That's another equation. But we derived Eq A and Eq B from equalities. However, Eq A and Eq B are linear equations that the circumcenter P must satisfy, and they are derived from two equalities (|PA|=|PC| and |PA|=|PD|). But note that we used |PA|=|PC| and |PA|=|PD|, which gives two equations. Since P is intersection of perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD, but we used distances to A and C, and A and D, which indeed yields linear equations because the quadratic terms cancel. So these two linear equations define the perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD (but not necessarily, because for a segment, the perpendicular bisector is the set of points equidistant; it's a line). So indeed, the equations we derived are the equations of the perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD, but they are not necessarily perpendicular to AC and AD? Actually they are lines; we derived them from |PA|^2 = |PC|^2, which simplifies to linear equation; that's the perpendicular bisector of AC. So it's correct. So we have two linear equations. However, we also have the third equality (|PC|=|PD|) which is automatically satisfied if both equalities with A hold, but we can use any two. So we can solve for P from Eq A and Eq B. That might be simpler than intersecting with vertical line. But note that Eq A and Eq B are two linear equations in x and y. We can solve for (x,y) directly. Let v = P - A. Then |A + t v|^2 = r1^2.

Expand: |A|^2 + 2 t A·v + t^2 |v|^2 = r1^2. Since |A|^2 = r1^2, we get:

2 t A·v + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 => t (2 A·v + t |v|^2) = 0. So t = 0 (A) or t = -2 A·v / |v|^2. So t_E = -2 A·v / |v|^2.

Similarly, for Γ: Let w = P - A. Then point F is A + t_F * w, with t_F ≠ 0. Condition: |(A + t w) - N| = r2. Since A is on Γ, we have |A - N| = r2. So we have:

2 t (A - N)·w + t^2 |w|^2 = 0 => t (2 (A - N)·w + t |w|^2) = 0. So t_F = -2 (A - N)·w / |w|^2.

Thus we have E and F expressed.

Given M = (x_M,0), N = (x_N,0), with x_M < x_N. Then:

MA^2 = x_M^2 + y0^2 = r1^2  (1)
NA^2 = (x_N)^2 + y0^2 = r2^2  (2)
MN = d = x_N - x_M > 0.

We can solve for x_M and x_N. Subtract (2)-(1): x_N^2 - x_M^2 = r2^2 - r1^2. So (x_N - x_M)(x_N + x_M) = r2^2 - r1^2. Let S = x_N + x_M. Then d * S = r2^2 - r1^2. So S = (r2^2 - r1^2) / d.

Then we also have (x_N - x_M)^2 = d^2. And we have x_M^2 + y0^2 = r1^2. So we can find x_M and x_N individually:

x_M = (S - d)/2 = ( (r2^2 - r1^2)/d - d ) / 2 = ( (r2^2 - r1^2) - d^2 ) / (2d).
x_N = (S + d)/2 = ( (r2^2 - r1^2)/d + d ) / 2 = ( (r2^2 - r1^2) + d^2 ) / (2d).

Thus we get expressions similar to earlier but with signs. Previously we had M = (0,0), N = (d,0) and A = (h,k) with h = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/(2d). That's essentially the same if we translate. So both coordinate systems are equivalent.

Now, I'll choose a coordinate system that seems to simplify P. Since A is at (0,y0) and C and D are on x-axis, then P's x-coordinate is something like ( (x_M - r1) + (x_N + r2) ) / 2. That's a bit messy but maybe simplifies. Let's compute with the new coordinates.

Set M = (m,0), N = (n,0) with m < n. Then:

C = (m - r1, 0)
D = (n + r2, 0)

A = (0, a) with a > 0.

Now we need P, the circumcenter of ACD. We can compute using perpendicular bisectors.

The perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line x = ( (m - r1) + (n + r2) ) / 2 = ( (m + n) + (r2 - r1) ) / 2.

The perpendicular bisector of AC (or AD). Let's use AC.

A = (0,a), C = (m - r1, 0). Midpoint: ((m - r1)/2, a/2). Slope of AC: (0 - a) / (m - r1 - 0) = -a / (m - r1). The perpendicular slope is (m - r1) / a. So equation:

(y - a/2) = ( (m - r1) / a ) * (x - (m - r1)/2).

Then P's x-coordinate is as above. So we can compute y_P.

Alternatively, use the distance equalities to derive linear equations. That might be easier. Since we have coordinates, we can compute directly.

Let’s denote:

x_P = ( (m - r1) + (n + r2) ) / 2 = (m + n + r2 - r1) / 2.

Now compute y_P from |P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2. That yields:

(x_P)^2 + (y_P - a)^2 = (x_P - (m - r1))^2 + (y_P - 0)^2.

Expand:

x_P^2 + y_P^2 - 2a y_P + a^2 = (x_P - (m - r1))^2 + y_P^2.

Cancel y_P^2. So:

x_P^2 - 2a y_P + a^2 = x_P^2 - 2x_P (m - r1) + (m - r1)^2.

Cancel x_P^2:

-2a y_P + a^2 = -2x_P (m - r1) + (m - r1)^2.

Thus:

-2a y_P = -2x_P (m - r1) + (m - r1)^2 - a^2.

Multiply by -1: 2a y_P = 2x_P (m - r1) - (m - r1)^2 + a^2.

So:

y_P = [ 2x_P (m - r1) - (m - r1)^2 + a^2 ] / (2a).

Now we also have relation: a^2 = r1^2 - m^2. Because MA^2 = m^2 + a^2 = r1^2. So a^2 = r1^2 - m^2. Similarly, a^2 = r2^2 - n^2. So that's consistent.

We can also compute using AD instead. Might simplify later.

Given M = (0,0), N = (d,0), P = (x_P, y_P). Then PN vector = (d - x_P, -y_P). A direction vector perpendicular to PN is (y_P, d - x_P). So altitude from M (0,0) is param λ (y_P, d - x_P). - d^2 - d r2 - r1 r2 + r1^2 + d^2 - r1^2 - r2^2 = (-d^2 + d^2) + (- d r2) + (- r1 r2) + (r1^2 - r1^2) - r2^2 = - d r2 - r1 r2 - r2^2.

Thus (A - N)·v = - ( d r2 + r1 r2 + r2^2 ) / 2 = - r2 ( d + r1 + r2 ) / 2.

So we get:

(A - N)·v = - r2 (d + r1 + r2) / 2.

That is a neat simplification. Let's double-check with numeric example: r1=3, r2=5, d=4 → (A - N)·v = -5 * (4+3+5)/2 = -5 * 12/2 = -5 * 6 = -30. Earlier we computed (A - N)·v = -30, matches. So good.

Thus we have:

A·v = - r1 * ( (d + r1 + r2) / 2 ) = - r1 (d + r1 + r2) / 2.

Similarly, (A - N)·v = - r2 (d + r1 + r2) / 2.

Let’s denote:

Eq1: (a v_x) x + (2k + a v_y) y = 0.
Eq2: (b v_x) x + (2k + b v_y) y = C2.

We can solve this 2x2 linear system. The determinant D = (a v_x)(2k + b v_y) - (b v_x)(2k + a v_y) = v_x [ a (2k + b v_y) - b (2k + a v_y) ] = v_x [ 2k a + a b v_y - 2k b - a b v_y ] = v_x * 2k (a - b). So D = 2k v_x (a - b). That's nice.

Thus D is non-zero (assuming v_x ≠ 0, a ≠ b). v_x = ((r2 - r1)S)/(2d) is non-zero because r2 > r1. a and b are different because r1 ≠ r2. So D ≠ 0.

Then we can solve for x and y:

x = ( (0)*(2k + b v_y) - (2k + a v_y) * C2 ) / D? Actually using Cramer's rule:

x = det( [0, (2k + a v_y); C2, (2k + b v_y)] ) / D = (0 * (2k + b v_y) - (2k + a v_y) * C2) / D = - C2 (2k + a v_y) / D.

y = det( [a v_x, 0; b v_x, C2] ) / D = (a v_x * C2 - 0 * b v_x) / D = a v_x C2 / D.

Thus:

x = - C2 (2k + a v_y) / (2k v_x (a - b)).
y = a v_x C2 / (2k v_x (a - b)) = a C2 / (2k (a - b)).

So y is simplified: y = a C2 / (2k (a - b)). And x simplifies as well.

Now note that a - b = (r1 S - r2 S) / |v|^2 = S (r1 - r2) / |v|^2 = - S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2. So a - b is negative. That's okay.

But we also have that a = (r1 S) / |v|^2. So a is known in terms of |v|^2. Similarly b. So we can compute a C2 / (2k (a - b)). This seems messy, but maybe it simplifies nicely because many terms cancel. Let's try to compute O explicitly.

First, compute C2 in terms of b and |v|^2. Since A·v = - r1 S / 2, then:

b (A·v) = b * (- r1 S / 2) = - (r1 S b) / 2.

Then C2 = - (r1 S b) / 2 + (b^2 |v|^2) / 2 = (1/2) ( b^2 |v|^2 - r1 S b ).

Now plug b = (r2 S) / |v|^2. Then b^2 |v|^2 = (r2^2 S^2) / |v|^4 * |v|^2 = r2^2 S^2 / |v|^2. Also, r1 S b = r1 S * (r2 S / |v|^2) = r1 r2 S^2 / |v|^2. So:

C2 = (1/2) ( (r2^2 S^2 / |v|^2) - (r1 r2 S^2 / |v|^2) ) = (1/2) * (S^2 r2 (r2 - r1) / |v|^2) = (S^2 r2 (r2 - r1)) / (2 |v|^2). This matches earlier expression with r2(r2 - r1) (since r2 - r1 > 0). So C2 = (S^2 r2 (r2 - r1)) / (2 |v|^2). Good.

Now a = (r1 S) / |v|^2. So a - b = S (r1 - r2) / |v|^2 = - S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2.

Thus (a - b) = - S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2.

Now compute y = a C2 / (2k (a - b)). Substitute:

a C2 = (r1 S / |v|^2) * (S^2 r2 (r2 - r1) / (2 |v|^2)) = r1 r2 S^3 (r2 - r1) / (2 |v|^4). Then divide by (2k (a - b)). So:

y = [ r1 r2 S^3 (r2 - r1) / (2 |v|^4) ] / [ 2k * ( - S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2 ) ] = [ r1 r2 S^3 (r2 - r1) / (2 |v|^4) ] * [ 1 / (2k * (- S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2) ) ] = [ r1 r2 S^3 (r2 - r1) / (2 |v|^4) ] * [ |v|^2 / (2k * (- S (r2 - r1)) ] = [ r1 r2 S^3 (r2 - r1) * |v|^2 ] / [ 2 |v|^4 * 2k * (- S (r2 - r1)) ] = [ r1 r2 S^3 (r2 - r1) |v|^2 ] / [ -4 k |v|^4 S (r2 - r1) ].

Cancel (r2 - r1) (assuming non-zero). Cancel one S: S^3 / S = S^2. So:

y = [ r1 r2 S^2 |v|^2 ] / [ -4 k |v|^4 ] = [ r1 r2 S^2 ] / [ -4 k |v|^2 ] = - (r1 r2 S^2) / (4 k |v|^2).

Thus y = - (r1 r2 S^2) / (4 k |v|^2). That's a nice expression.

Now compute x = - C2 (2k + a v_y) / (2k v_x (a - b)). But we might also find a simpler expression for O. Note that y is independent of v_x, v_y. So O's y-coordinate is determined. Now we can also compute O's x-coordinate. But perhaps we can find a more elegant approach: The circumcenter O of BEF can be expressed as the midpoint of the segment connecting the orthocenters of something? Not.

Alternatively, we can compute O by solving the linear system using Cramer's rule, but we already got y. We also need to compute det(O - H, v) and R|v|. Since we have expressions for O and H, we can compute. But we also need to compute R = |O - B|. That seems messy.

Maybe we can prove tangency by showing that H is the orthocenter of triangle PMN, and that line through H parallel to AP is the polar of A with respect to the circle (BEF), which would be tangent if A lies on the circle. But A does not lie on that circle, so not.

Wait, perhaps we misinterpreted the problem: "Prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF." That's what we have. So we need to show that the line L (through H parallel to AP) touches the circle (BEF). That is, L is a tangent line to that circle. Since L passes through H, but H is not necessarily on the circle. So we need to show that L is tangent, meaning that it intersects the circle at exactly one point. That point is the point of tangency. So L is not necessarily passing through a vertex of the triangle BEF, it's just a line.

So we need to prove that distance from center O to L equals radius. That's a standard condition. So if we can compute O and then compute the distance, we can verify equality. The challenge is to compute O in a manageable way. Perhaps we can find that O lies on the line through M (origin) perpendicular to something, and that O's coordinates can be expressed nicely using a and b and the parameters. Let's try to compute O directly using the formula for the circumcenter in terms of coordinates. There's a known formula: For three points P1,P2,P3, the circumcenter is given by:

O = ( \frac{ |P1|^2 (P2 - P3) + |P2|^2 (P3 - P1) + |P3|^2 (P1 - P2) }{ 2 (P1 × P2 + P2 × P3 + P3 × P1) } )? That's for complex numbers. In vector form, it's easier to solve the linear system.

We have B, E, F. B = (h,-k). E = A + a v, F = A + b v. So we can compute O by solving:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 → O·(E - B) = (|E|^2 - |B|^2)/2.
|O - B|^2 = |O - F|^2 → O·(F - B) = (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2.

That's two linear equations. So we can solve for O. We already did that and got expressions for x and y. But we need to also incorporate the fact that O is the circumcenter, so it's uniquely determined. That's fine. Now we can compute O - H and then det(O - H, v). That seems doable. Let's continue with that approach.

We have M = (0,0). So coordinates.

We have:

u = E - B = (a v_x, 2k + a v_y)
w = F - B = (b v_x, 2k + b v_y)

We also have a = r1 S / |v|^2, b = r2 S / |v|^2.

We derived:

x_O = - C2 (2k + a v_y) / (2k v_x (a - b))
y_O = a C2 / (2k (a - b))

But we also have C2 expressed as (S^2 r2 (r2 - r1)) / (2 |v|^2). And a - b = - S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2.

Now compute y_O:

a C2 / (2k (a - b)) = a C2 / (2k * ( - S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2 ) ) = - a C2 |v|^2 / (2k S (r2 - r1)). Then plug a and C2.

But earlier we simplified to y_O = - (r1 r2 S^2) / (4 k |v|^2). That seems correct after simplification. Let's re-evaluate that simplification carefully.

We had:

y_O = a C2 / (2k (a - b)). But note that in Cramer's rule we derived y = a v_x C2 / D, with D = 2k v_x (a - b). So y = a v_x C2 / (2k v_x (a - b)) = a C2 / (2k (a - b)). So that's correct. Then we computed a C2 / (2k (a - b)). That's y_O.

Now a - b = (r1 S / |v|^2) - (r2 S / |v|^2) = S (r1 - r2) / |v|^2 = - S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2.

Thus:

y_O = \frac{a C2}{2k} * \frac{1}{a - b} = \frac{a C2}{2k} * \frac{1}{- S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2} = - \frac{a C2 |v|^2}{2k S (r2 - r1)}.

Now plug a = r1 S / |v|^2:

a C2 |v|^2 = (r1 S / |v|^2) * C2 * |v|^2 = r1 S C2.

So y_O = - \frac{r1 S C2}{2k S (r2 - r1)} = - \frac{r1 C2}{2k (r2 - r1)}.

Now C2 = (S^2 r2 (r2 - r1)) / (2 |v|^2). So:

y_O = - \frac{r1}{2k (r2 - r1)} * \frac{S^2 r2 (r2 - r1)}{2 |v|^2} = - \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{4 k |v|^2}.

Thus y_O = - \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{4 k |v|^2}. Good.

Now we also need x_O. Compute x_O from Cramer's rule:

Plug 1/(a - b) = - |v|^2 / (S (r2 - r1)). So:

Now we can use the relationships (1) and (2) to simplify.

(r1 - m) = \frac{a^2}{r1 + m} (since (r1 - m)(r1 + m) = a^2).
(r2 + n) = \frac{a^2}{r2 - n} (since (r2 - n)(r2 + n) = a^2). So product = \frac{a^4}{(r1 + m)(r2 - n)}. Then |v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2}{a^2} * \frac{a^4}{(r1 + m)(r2 - n)} = r1 r2 * \frac{a^2}{(r1 + m)(r2 - n)}. That's not obviously simpler.

Alternatively, we can compute |v|^2 directly using the expression we derived with (r1 - m)(r2 + n) and then substitute m and n in terms of a, r1, r2. That seems messy. But perhaps we don't need to compute |v|^2 explicitly; we can use the fact that a = r1 S / |v|^2, b = r2 S / |v|^2, and we have expressions for a and b in terms of m,n etc. That might lead to simplifications when computing O's coordinates. However, the determinant condition might simplify with a lot of cancellations.

Given the difficulty, maybe we should consider a different analytic approach: Use coordinates where M and N are at (0,0) and (d,0) as before, but then also use vector notation to directly compute the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) and then relate to the line L. Let's step back and think about the problem in terms of inversion or spiral similarity. So indeed.

Now note that H is orthocenter of PMN. In this example, P = (3,-3), M=(0,0), N=(4,0). So H = (3,-1). That's (x_P, something). So H lies on the vertical line x=3. The line AP is from (0,3) to (3,-3). Now can we prove that L is tangent without solving for O? Thus we need a direct approach. I think analytic geometry is the way, but we need to manage the algebra. Let's attempt to compute O explicitly using the linear equations, but then also compute H and v. Perhaps we can simplify by choosing a coordinate system where M = (0,0) and N = (d,0), and also use that A = (h,k) with h and k expressed. Then we can compute P, v, a, b, C2. Then compute O. We can try to simplify by noting that many expressions involve S and |v|^2, and we might find that det(O - H, v) simplifies to something like (r1 r2 S^2) / (2k) * something? Let's attempt.

First, compute |v|^2 in terms of r1,r2,d,k,h. We already have an expression from the circumradius: |v|^2 = \frac{AC^2 \cdot AD^2}{4 k^2}. We computed AC^2 and AD^2 in the M=(0,0) coordinate. Let's compute them again with M=(0,0), N=(d,0), A=(h,k). Then:

C = (-r1,0). So AC^2 = (h + r1)^2 + k^2. Using MA^2 = h^2 + k^2 = r1^2, we got AC^2 = 2 r1 (h + r1). That's valid.

D = (d + r2,0). So AD^2 = (h - (d + r2))^2 + k^2. Using NA^2 = (h - d)^2 + k^2 = r2^2. Then AD^2 = 2 r2 (r2 - (h - d)). That's (h - d) is something, so AD^2 = 2 r2 (r2 - h + d). That's AD^2 = 2 r2 (d + r2 - h). That's the same as before. So AD^2 = 2 r2 (S - r1?) Actually S = d + r1 + r2. Then r2 - h + d = d + r2 - h. That's not obviously S. But we can compute h expression.

Now |v|^2 = \frac{2 r1 (h + r1) * 2 r2 (d + r2 - h)}{4 k^2} = \frac{4 r1 r2 (h + r1)(d + r2 - h)}{4 k^2} = \frac{r1 r2 (h + r1)(d + r2 - h)}{k^2}.

We also have k^2 = r1^2 - h^2. So we can compute (h + r1)(d + r2 - h). That seems messy. But earlier we derived a simpler expression using AC^2 and AD^2 with the symmetric coordinate. However, we can also compute |v|^2 using the formula R_ACD = \frac{AC \cdot AD}{2k}. That's fine. So |v|^2 = \frac{AC^2 \cdot AD^2}{4 k^2}. So we have that.

Now a = r1 S / |v|^2, b = r2 S / |v|^2. So a and b are expressed.

Now compute C2 = (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2. |F|^2 = |A + b v|^2 = |A|^2 + 2 b (A·v) + b^2 |v|^2 = r1^2 + 2 b (A·v) + b^2 |v|^2. Since A·v = - r1 S / 2, we have 2 b (A·v) = - b r1 S. So |F|^2 = r1^2 - b r1 S + b^2 |v|^2. Then |F|^2 - |B|^2 = - b r1 S + b^2 |v|^2. So C2 = (- b r1 S + b^2 |v|^2) / 2.

Now substitute b = r2 S / |v|^2. Then:

b^2 |v|^2 = (r2^2 S^2 / |v|^4) * |v|^2 = r2^2 S^2 / |v|^2.
b r1 S = (r2 S / |v|^2) * r1 S = r1 r2 S^2 / |v|^2.

Thus C2 = ( - r1 r2 S^2 / |v|^2 + r2^2 S^2 / |v|^2 ) / 2 = \frac{S^2 r2 (r2 - r1)}{2 |v|^2}.

That's consistent.

Now we also need v_x and v_y. v = P - A. P is circumcenter of ACD. Let's compute P explicitly in this coordinate. We have:

M = (0,0), N = (d,0). A = (h,k). C = (-r1,0). D = (d + r2, 0). So:

x_P = (C_x + D_x)/2 = ( -r1 + d + r2 ) / 2 = (d + r2 - r1)/2.
y_P from equation (h + r1) x_P + k y_P = 0 (derived from |PA|^2 = |PC|^2). That gives:

y_P = - (h + r1) x_P / k.

Thus v = (x_P - h, y_P - k).

Now compute v_x = (d + r2 - r1)/2 - h. We derived earlier v_x = ((r2 - r1)S) / (2d). That's a nice expression. Let's confirm:

h = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d). Then v_x = (d + r2 - r1)/2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d). Multiply numerator and denominator? Compute:

v_x = \frac{1}{2} \left( d + r2 - r1 - \frac{d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2}{d} \right) = \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{d(d + r2 - r1) - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)}{d} \right) = \frac{1}{2d} \left( d^2 + d r2 - d r1 - d^2 - r1^2 + r2^2 \right) = \frac{1}{2d} \left( d(r2 - r1) - (r1^2 - r2^2) \right). Now r1^2 - r2^2 = -(r2^2 - r1^2). So - (r1^2 - r2^2) = r2^2 - r1^2. But we have d(r2 - r1) - (r1^2 - r2^2). That's d(r2 - r1) + (r2^2 - r1^2) = (r2 - r1)(d + r1 + r2) = (r2 - r1) S. So v_x = \frac{1}{2d} * ( (r2 - r1) S ) = \frac{(r2 - r1) S}{2d}. So indeed v_x = (r2 - r1) S / (2d). Good.

Now v_y = y_P - k = - (h + r1) x_P / k - k.

We can try to simplify v_y. We have x_P = (d + r2 - r1)/2. And h + r1 = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) + r1 = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2 + 2d r1) / (2d) = ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = ( (d + r1 - r2)(d + r1 + r2) ) / (2d) = ( (d + r1 - r2) S ) / (2d). That's positive. So:

(h + r1) x_P = \frac{ ( (d + r1 - r2) S ) }{2d} * \frac{ (d + r2 - r1) }{2} = \frac{ S (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) }{4d}. Then (h + r1) x_P / k = \frac{ S (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) }{4d k}. So v_y = - \frac{ S (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) }{4d k} - k.

Thus v_y = -k - \frac{ S (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) }{4d k}. That's not obviously simplifying.

Now note that (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) = d^2 - (r2 - r1)^2 = Δ2. And we have k^2 = \frac{Δ1 Δ2}{4 d^2}. So perhaps we can combine these to get v_y in a nicer form. Let's compute the term \frac{ S Δ2 }{4d k}. That is \frac{ S Δ2 }{4d k}. Multiply numerator and denominator: \frac{ S Δ2 }{4d k}. Since k^2 = Δ1 Δ2 / (4 d^2), we have k = \frac{ \sqrt{Δ1 Δ2} }{2 d}. Then \frac{ S Δ2 }{4d k} = \frac{ S Δ2 }{4d} * \frac{2 d}{ \sqrt{Δ1 Δ2} } = \frac{ S Δ2 * 2 d }{4 d \sqrt{Δ1 Δ2}} = \frac{ S Δ2 }{2 \sqrt{Δ1 Δ2}} = \frac{ S \sqrt{Δ2} }{2 \sqrt{Δ1} }.

That's messy. But maybe we don't need v_y explicitly if we can compute (2k + a v_y) in a different way. Let's compute a v_y:

a v_y = a * ( -k - \frac{ S Δ2 }{4d k} ) = - a k - a \frac{ S Δ2 }{4d k}. Then 2k + a v_y = 2k - a k - a \frac{ S Δ2 }{4d k} = k (2 - a) - a \frac{ S Δ2 }{4d k}. That seems messy.

But perhaps we can compute x_O directly using another approach: Instead of solving the linear system with u and w, we could find O by using the fact that O is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. That seemed to give simpler expressions for a and b and v_x, v_y? Let's explore that coordinate.

Set midpoint of AB as origin: A = (0,a), B = (0,-a). M = (m,0), N = (n,0) with m < n. Then C = (m - r1, 0), D = (n + r2, 0). Then P's x-coordinate is (m + n + (r2 - r1))/2. Now also v_x = x_P - 0 = x_P = (m + n + (r2 - r1))/2. That's messy. But maybe we can also compute v_y = y_P - a. And we have y_P from the perpendicular bisector condition. Let's compute y_P in this coordinate.

Now we need y_P. Use |PA|^2 = |PC|^2:

(x_P)^2 + (y_P - a)^2 = (x_P - (m - r1))^2 + (y_P)^2.

Expand:

x_P^2 + y_P^2 - 2a y_P + a^2 = x_P^2 - 2 x_P (m - r1) + (m - r1)^2 + y_P^2.

Cancel x_P^2 + y_P^2:

-2a y_P + a^2 = -2 x_P (m - r1) + (m - r1)^2.

Thus:

2a y_P = 2 x_P (m - r1) - (m - r1)^2 + a^2. So y_P = \frac{2 x_P (m - r1) - (m - r1)^2 + a^2}{2a}.

Now also we have m^2 + a^2 = r1^2. So (m - r1)^2 = m^2 - 2 m r1 + r1^2. Then (m - r1)^2 + a^2 = m^2 + a^2 - 2 m r1 + r1^2 = r1^2 - 2 m r1 + r1^2 = 2 r1^2 - 2 m r1 = 2 r1 (r1 - m). That's AC^2. That's consistent. So we have an expression for y_P.

Now v_x = x_P, v_y = y_P - a.

Now also S = d + r1 + r2. But d = n - m. So S = n - m + r1 + r2. That's similar.

Given M = (m,0), N = (n,0), P = (x_P, y_P). Then PN vector = (n - x_P, -y_P). A direction perpendicular to PN is (y_P, n - x_P). So altitude from M (m,0) param: (m,0) + λ (y_P, n - x_P). That's a formula. So H_y = \frac{(x_P - m)(n - x_P)}{y_P}.

Now note that x_P - m = (m + n + (r2 - r1))/2 - m = ( -m + n + (r2 - r1))/2 = (n - m + (r2 - r1))/2 = (d + (r2 - r1))/2. Thus H_y = \frac{ Δ2 / 4 }{ y_P } = \frac{ Δ2 }{ 4 y_P }. Since y_P is negative (as we will see). So H_y = \frac{ Δ2 }{ 4 y_P }. This is independent of m,n signs? It uses d = n - m, and Δ2 = d^2 - (r2 - r1)^2. That's a positive number. So H_y is something.

Now also note that from earlier in the M=(0,0) coordinate we had H_y = \frac{ x_P (d - x_P) }{ y_P }? That was with M and N symmetric about origin. That's a different expression.

Now this coordinate seems promising because we have a nice expression for H_y in terms of Δ2 and y_P. Also, we have v_x and v_y expressed. But we still need to compute O.

Maybe we can find that O's y-coordinate is something like - \frac{ r1 r2 S^2 }{ 4 a^2 } / |v|^2? Actually we derived y_O = - (r1 r2 S^2) / (4k |v|^2). That's general. In this coordinate, k = a. So y_O = - (r1 r2 S^2) / (4 a |v|^2). That's okay.

Now we also need x_O. That's messy. But perhaps we can find an expression for O's x-coordinate using the perpendicular bisector of EF. That line is perpendicular to v. So O satisfies (O - M_EF)·v = 0. M_EF is midpoint of EF, which is A + ((a+b)/2) v. That's a point on AP. So the line is perpendicular to v. That's a linear equation. The other equation is from perpendicular bisector of BE. That's another linear equation. So O is determined. Maybe we can solve for O in this coordinate and then compute det(O - H, v) and compare with R|v|. That seems heavy but perhaps we can find a simplification: Since we have expressions for a, b, and |v|^2, maybe we can compute O in terms of A, v, and the parameters. Let's try to solve the linear system in a vector form.

We have B, E, F. Let’s denote vectors in ℝ^2. We can write O as unknown.

Equations:

(1) |O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 → (O - B)·(O - B) = (O - E)·(O - E) → -2 O·B + |B|^2 = -2 O·E + |E|^2 → 2 O·(E - B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2.

(2) |O - B|^2 = |O - F|^2 → 2 O·(F - B) = |F|^2 - |B|^2.

Now |E|^2 - |B|^2 = 2 b (A·v) + b^2 |v|^2, because E = A + b v. Similarly, |F|^2 - |B|^2 = 2 a (A·v) + a^2 |v|^2, but careful: F = A + a v? Actually we defined a = t_F (the parameter for F) and b = t_E (the parameter for E). But earlier we had E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v. But we need to be consistent: In the M=(0,0) coordinate, we had E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v. Then a = t_F (for F) and b = t_E (for E). But in our earlier formulas, we used a = r1 S / |v|^2 (for E) and b = r2 S / |v|^2 (for F). That's fine. But then the RHS for equation with F (which we used for C2) is (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2. That's C2 = (2 a (A·v) + a^2 |v|^2)/2? Actually |F|^2 - |B|^2 = 2 a (A·v) + a^2 |v|^2. That's if we set a = t_F (the parameter for F). So we need to define:

Let α = t_E (for E) and β = t_F (for F). But we earlier derived a = r1 S / |v|^2, b = r2 S / |v|^2. Which is which? We computed E and F using param with v = P - A. We had:

E = A + t_E v, with t_E = -2 (A·v) / |v|^2. Since A·v = - r1 S / 2, then t_E = -2 * (- r1 S / 2) / |v|^2 = (r1 S) / |v|^2. So t_E = r1 S / |v|^2.

Similarly, t_F = -2 (A - N)·v / |v|^2. Since (A - N)·v = - r2 S / 2, then t_F = -2 * (- r2 S / 2) / |v|^2 = (r2 S) / |v|^2. So t_F = r2 S / |v|^2. So indeed E corresponds to parameter r1 S / |v|^2, F corresponds to r2 S / |v|^2. So we have:

E = A + (r1 S / |v|^2) v.
F = A + (r2 S / |v|^2) v.

Thus we can denote:

u1 = r1 S / |v|^2 (for E), u2 = r2 S / |v|^2 (for F). So the larger is u2 (since r2 > r1). That's fine.

Now compute |E|^2 - |B|^2 = 2 u1 (A·v) + u1^2 |v|^2. Since A·v = - r1 S / 2, then:

2 u1 (A·v) = 2 * (r1 S / |v|^2) * (- r1 S / 2) = - r1^2 S^2 / |v|^2.
u1^2 |v|^2 = (r1^2 S^2 / |v|^4) * |v|^2 = r1^2 S^2 / |v|^2.
So |E|^2 - |B|^2 = - r1^2 S^2 / |v|^2 + r1^2 S^2 / |v|^2 = 0. So indeed the RHS for equation involving E is 0. That's a crucial simplification: O·(E - B) = 0. So O is orthogonal to (E - B). This is independent of coordinate. So that's a linear equation.

Now for F: |F|^2 - |B|^2 = 2 u2 (A·v) + u2^2 |v|^2. u2 = r2 S / |v|^2. Then 2 u2 (A·v) = 2 * (r2 S / |v|^2) * (- r1 S / 2) = - r1 r2 S^2 / |v|^2. u2^2 |v|^2 = (r2^2 S^2 / |v|^4) * |v|^2 = r2^2 S^2 / |v|^2. So:

|F|^2 - |B|^2 = (r2^2 - r1 r2) S^2 / |v|^2 = r2 (r2 - r1) S^2 / |v|^2. Thus C2 = (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2 = \frac{r2 (r2 - r1) S^2}{2 |v|^2}.

Now also note that (E - B) = (A - B) + u1 v. But we already have that.

Now we have O·u = 0, where u = E - B. And O·w = C2, where w = F - B.

Now we can solve for O. That's what we did. The expression for y_O turned out to be something like - (r1 r2 S^2) / (4 k |v|^2). That seems correct. Let's derive again in a general coordinate with A = (h,k). But we need to confirm that the expression for y_O (the y-coordinate) is independent of the coordinate choice? Actually we solved the linear system with coordinates, so it's correct.

Now we need to compute det(O - H, v). Maybe we can find a relationship between O and H such that O - H is parallel to something like (k, something) and v is something, making the determinant equal to R|v|. Perhaps we can compute O explicitly by solving the system in terms of the vectors u and w. Since we have O·u = 0 and O·w = C2. This is a system of two linear equations in O (if we treat O as a vector). However, note that O is a point (position vector). The equations are dot products with vectors u and w. If u and w are linearly independent, we can solve for O as a linear combination of u and w. But we need to solve for O. Write O = α u + β w? Actually then O·u = α |u|^2 + β (u·w). That's not directly α and β. So better to treat as linear equations: u^T O = 0, w^T O = C2. This is a 2x2 linear system. The solution O is given by:

O = \frac{1}{det} \begin{pmatrix} 0 & -C2 \\ C2 & 0 \end{pmatrix}? Actually we can solve using Cramer's rule. The determinant of the coefficient matrix is det([u; w]) = u_x w_y - u_y w_x. So O = (0 * w_perp - C2 * u_perp?) Actually we need to solve:

u_x x + u_y y = 0.
w_x x + w_y y = C2.

Then solution:

x = \frac{0 * w_y - u_y * C2}{u_x w_y - u_y w_x} = - \frac{u_y C2}{D},
y = \frac{u_x * C2 - 0 * w_x}{D} = \frac{u_x C2}{D},

where D = u_x w_y - u_y w_x = det([u; w]). That's a nice formula. So we can compute O in terms of u and w. Then O - H can be expressed.

Now H is (x_P, y_H). We have expressions for u and w. Let's compute u = E - B and w = F - B. But perhaps we can simplify by noting that u is proportional to something like (v_x, something) and w is proportional to (v_x, something). Let's compute u and w explicitly in terms of v and A - B. We have:

A - B = (0, 2k) if A = (h,k), B = (h,-k). So A - B is vertical. So:

u = (A - B) + u1 v = (0, 2k) + u1 v. Similarly, w = (0, 2k) + u2 v.

But careful: E = A + u1 v, so E - B = (A - B) + u1 v. That's correct. So u = (0, 2k) + u1 v. w = (0, 2k) + u2 v.

Now v is P - A. We know v_x and v_y.

Now we can compute D = det(u, w) = det( (0, 2k) + u1 v, (0, 2k) + u2 v ). Since determinant is bilinear and alternating, we can expand:

det(u, w) = det( (0, 2k), (0, 2k) ) + u1 det( (0, 2k), v ) + u2 det( (0, 2k), v )? Wait careful: det(A + u1 v, B + u2 v) = det(A, B) + u1 det(A, v) + u2 det(B, v) + u1 u2 det(v, v). Since det(v, v) = 0. So:

det(u, w) = det( (0, 2k), (0, 2k) ) + u1 det( (0, 2k), v ) + u2 det( (0, 2k), v )? Actually B = (0, 2k) also. So both A and B are (0, 2k). So:

Thus D = (u1 + u2) * det( (0, 2k), v ). That's nice.

But we also have H expressed in terms of x_P, y_H. And we have expressions for x_P and y_H from earlier.

Maybe we can find a more synthetic relationship: The line through H parallel to AP is L. Consider the circle with diameter MN. Its center is midpoint of MN. The line AP is a line that is the radical axis of something? Not.

Given the complexity, I think it's best to proceed with an analytic solution using coordinates where M = (0,0) and N = (d,0). Then compute P, v, a, b, C2, then compute O using the linear system. Then compute det(O - H, v). Then compute R^2 = |O - B|^2. Then show equality. That is a lot of algebra, but with careful simplification, it should be doable. I'll try to do it step by step, and see if cancellations occur.

First, define:

M = (0,0)
N = (d,0)
A = (h,k)
B = (h,-k)

We have relations:

(1) h^2 + k^2 = r1^2
(2) (h - d)^2 + k^2 = r2^2

Thus:

h^2 = r1^2 - k^2
(h - d)^2 = r2^2 - k^2

Expand (h - d)^2 = h^2 - 2hd + d^2. So:

h^2 - 2hd + d^2 = r2^2 - k^2

Substitute h^2 = r1^2 - k^2:

r1^2 - k^2 - 2hd + d^2 = r2^2 - k^2 → r1^2 - 2hd + d^2 = r2^2 → 2hd = r1^2 + d^2 - r2^2 → h = (r1^2 + d^2 - r2^2) / (2d). This is the same as before. So we have h expressed.

Now we need P. As before, x_P = (C_x + D_x)/2 = ( -r1 + (d + r2) ) / 2 = (d + r2 - r1)/2.
y_P from |PA|^2 = |PC|^2 → (x_P - h)^2 + (y_P - k)^2 = (x_P + r1)^2 + y_P^2 (since C = (-r1,0)). This yields:

(x_P - h)^2 + y_P^2 - 2k y_P + k^2 = x_P^2 + 2 r1 x_P + r1^2 + y_P^2

Cancel y_P^2 and x_P^2? Actually (x_P - h)^2 = x_P^2 - 2 x_P h + h^2. So:

x_P^2 - 2 x_P h + h^2 - 2k y_P + k^2 = x_P^2 + 2 r1 x_P + r1^2.

Cancel x_P^2:

-2 x_P h + h^2 - 2k y_P + k^2 = 2 r1 x_P + r1^2

Thus:

-2k y_P = 2 r1 x_P + r1^2 + 2 x_P h - h^2 - k^2

But h^2 + k^2 = r1^2, so -h^2 - k^2 = -r1^2. Then:

-2k y_P = 2 r1 x_P + r1^2 + 2 x_P h - r1^2 = 2 r1 x_P + 2 x_P h = 2 x_P (r1 + h). So:

-2k y_P = 2 x_P (r1 + h) → y_P = - \frac{ x_P (r1 + h) }{ k }.

That's the same as earlier. So y_P = - (x_P (r1 + h)) / k. That's a linear relation. Good.

Now compute v = P - A = (x_P - h, y_P - k). So:

v_x = x_P - h.
v_y = y_P - k = - \frac{x_P (r1 + h)}{k} - k.

Now we also have expressions for x_P, h, etc. We can compute v_x in terms of d, r1, r2. We already did: v_x = ((r2 - r1) S) / (2d). That's nice. So:

v_x = \frac{ (r2 - r1) S }{ 2d }.

Now compute (r1 + h). Using h expression:

r1 + h = r1 + \frac{r1^2 + d^2 - r2^2}{2d} = \frac{2 r1 d + r1^2 + d^2 - r2^2}{2d}. That's not as simple. But we can also compute (r1 + h) in terms of S and d? Let's compute S = d + r1 + r2. Then r1 + h = \frac{ (d + r1)^2 - r2^2 }{2d}? Actually earlier we derived that (r1 + h) = ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d). Let's verify:

We have h = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d). Then r1 + h = r1 + (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = (2 r1 d + d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = ( (d^2 + 2 r1 d + r1^2) - r2^2 ) / (2d) = ( (d + r1)^2 - r2^2 ) / (2d). That's correct. So (r1 + h) = \frac{ (d + r1)^2 - r2^2 }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r1 - r2)(d + r1 + r2) }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r1 - r2) S }{2d}. That's positive because d + r1 - r2 > 0. Good.

Thus:

x_P (r1 + h) = \frac{ d + r2 - r1 }{2} * \frac{ (d + r1 - r2) S }{2d} = \frac{ S (d + r2 - r1)(d + r1 - r2) }{4 d}. 

Now (d + r2 - r1)(d + r1 - r2) = (d^2 - (r2 - r1)^2) = Δ2. So:

x_P (r1 + h) = \frac{ S Δ2 }{4 d}.

Then y_P = - \frac{ x_P (r1 + h) }{ k } = - \frac{ S Δ2 }{4 d k }.

Now compute v_y = y_P - k = - \frac{ S Δ2 }{4 d k } - k.

Thus v_y = -k - \frac{ S Δ2 }{4 d k }.

Now we have v_x, v_y, and also a and b (parameters for E and F). Recall:

a = t_F = (r2 S) / |v|^2.
b = t_E = (r1 S) / |v|^2.

We also need |v|^2. Let's compute |v|^2 using the expression from R_ACD. That gave:

|v|^2 = \frac{AC^2 \cdot AD^2}{4 k^2}. We have AC^2 = 2 r1 (h + r1) and AD^2 = 2 r2 (d + r2 - h). So:

|v|^2 = \frac{4 r1 r2 (h + r1)(d + r2 - h)}{4 k^2} = \frac{r1 r2 (h + r1)(d + r2 - h)}{k^2}. 

Now we need to simplify (h + r1)(d + r2 - h). We can compute that.

h + r1 = \frac{ (d + r1)^2 - r2^2 }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r1 - r2) S }{2d}. (We already have that.)
d + r2 - h = d + r2 - \frac{d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2}{2d} = \frac{2d^2 + 2d r2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)}{2d} = \frac{2d^2 + 2d r2 - d^2 - r1^2 + r2^2}{2d} = \frac{d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2}{2d}.

But we can also express d + r2 - h in terms of S and Δ2 maybe? Let's compute:

We have from NA^2: (h - d)^2 + k^2 = r2^2. But maybe we can find a similar factorization. Since h = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/(2d), then d + r2 - h = d + r2 - \frac{d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2}{2d} = \frac{2d^2 + 2d r2 - d^2 - r1^2 + r2^2}{2d} = \frac{d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2}{2d}. That's not obviously simplifying. But we can also compute (h + r1)(d + r2 - h) using the expressions we have. Perhaps we can find a nicer expression by noting that:

AC^2 = 2 r1 (h + r1) and AD^2 = 2 r2 (d + r2 - h). So the product is 4 r1 r2 (h + r1)(d + r2 - h). Then |v|^2 = (product)/(4 k^2). So that's fine. But we might want to compute |v|^2 explicitly in terms of d, r1, r2, k. But we have k^2 expressed. So we can compute:

|v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2}{k^2} * (h + r1)(d + r2 - h). So we need (h + r1)(d + r2 - h). Let's compute that.

First, compute (h + r1) = \frac{ (d + r1)^2 - r2^2 }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r1 - r2) S }{2d}. 
Now compute (d + r2 - h). We have:

d + r2 - h = d + r2 - \frac{d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2}{2d} = \frac{2d^2 + 2d r2 - d^2 - r1^2 + r2^2}{2d} = \frac{d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2}{2d}.

We can also try to express this as \frac{ (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 }{2d}? Let's compute (d + r2)^2 = d^2 + 2d r2 + r2^2. Then (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 = d^2 + 2d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2. That's exactly the numerator we have. So indeed:

d + r2 - h = \frac{ (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 }{2d}. But is that correct? Because we have d + r2 - h, not d + r2 + h. Let's compute:

(d + r2)^2 - r1^2 = (d^2 + 2d r2 + r2^2) - r1^2. That's not obviously equal to 2d (d + r2 - h). Let's compute 2d (d + r2 - h) = 2d^2 + 2d r2 - 2d h. And 2d h = d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2 (from h expression). So 2d^2 + 2d r2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) = d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2. That's the numerator we have. So indeed:

2d (d + r2 - h) = d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2 = (d + r2)^2 - r1^2. So:

d + r2 - h = \frac{ (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 }{2d}. That's a valid expression. So we have:

(d + r2 - h) = \frac{ (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r2 - r1)(d + r2 + r1) }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r2 - r1) S }{2d}. That's nice!

Thus:

h + r1 = \frac{ (d + r1 - r2) S }{2d}
d + r2 - h = \frac{ (d + r2 - r1) S }{2d}

Therefore:

(h + r1)(d + r2 - h) = \frac{ S^2 (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) }{4 d^2} = \frac{ S^2 Δ2 }{4 d^2}.

Now then |v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2}{k^2} * \frac{ S^2 Δ2 }{4 d^2} = \frac{r1 r2 S^2 Δ2}{4 d^2 k^2}.

But also we have k^2 expressed in terms of Δ1 Δ2: k^2 = \frac{Δ1 Δ2}{4 d^2}. So substitute:

|v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2 S^2 Δ2}{4 d^2 * \frac{Δ1 Δ2}{4 d^2}} = \frac{r1 r2 S^2 Δ2}{4 d^2} * \frac{4 d^2}{Δ1 Δ2} = \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1}. 

Wait careful:

|v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2 S^2 Δ2}{4 d^2 k^2} = \frac{r1 r2 S^2 Δ2}{4 d^2 * \frac{Δ1 Δ2}{4 d^2}} = \frac{r1 r2 S^2 Δ2}{4 d^2} * \frac{4 d^2}{Δ1 Δ2} = \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1}.

Thus:

|v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1}, where Δ1 = (r1 + r2)^2 - d^2. That's a nice simplification! So |v|^2 is expressed without k. Good.

Now we also have a = r1 S / |v|^2, b = r2 S / |v|^2. So:

a = r1 S / \left( \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1} \right) = \frac{r1 S * Δ1}{r1 r2 S^2} = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S}.
Similarly, b = r2 S / \left( \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1} \right) = \frac{r2 S * Δ1}{r1 r2 S^2} = \frac{Δ1}{r1 S}.

Thus:

a = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S}
b = \frac{Δ1}{r1 S}.

This is nice. So the parameters are expressed in terms of Δ1, r1, r2, S. Note that Δ1 = (r1 + r2)^2 - d^2 > 0.

Now we also have v_x = \frac{ (r2 - r1) S }{2d}. So that's fine.

Now we need to compute O (circumcenter of BEF). We have u = E - B, w = F - B. Let's compute u and w explicitly using the new expressions.

Recall:

A = (h,k). B = (h,-k). v = (v_x, v_y). Then:

E = A + a v, F = A + b v.

Thus:

u = E - B = (A - B) + a v = (0, 2k) + a v.
w = F - B = (0, 2k) + b v.

Now we have a and b expressions. Also v components.

Now we want to solve for O:

u_x x_O + u_y y_O = 0
w_x x_O + w_y y_O = C2, where C2 = (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2.

We already have expressions for C2 in terms of b and |v|^2, but we can also compute C2 using the formulas we derived: C2 = \frac{r2 (r2 - r1) S^2}{2 |v|^2}. But we have |v|^2 expressed, so we can simplify C2. Let's compute:

|v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1}. Then:

C2 = \frac{r2 (r2 - r1) S^2}{2 * \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1}} = \frac{r2 (r2 - r1) S^2 * Δ1}{2 r1 r2 S^2} = \frac{ (r2 - r1) Δ1 }{2 r1}.

Thus:

C2 = \frac{ (r2 - r1) Δ1 }{2 r1}.

That's a nice expression. Note that we also have a = Δ1/(r2 S), b = Δ1/(r1 S). So a and b involve S, but C2 does not involve S. That's interesting.

Now we also have u_x = a v_x, u_y = 2k + a v_y. Similarly w_x = b v_x, w_y = 2k + b v_y.

We need to compute D = det(u,w) = u_x w_y - u_y w_x. We derived earlier that D = 2k v_x (u1 - u2) but with our definitions (u1 = a, u2 = b). Let's confirm with general notation:

Let α = a, β = b. Then u = (α v_x, 2k + α v_y), w = (β v_x, 2k + β v_y). Then det = α v_x * (2k + β v_y) - (2k + α v_y) * β v_x = 2k v_x (α - β). So D = 2k v_x (α - β). Since α = a, β = b. And α - β = a - b = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S} - \frac{Δ1}{r1 S} = \frac{Δ1}{S} ( \frac{1}{r2} - \frac{1}{r1} ) = \frac{Δ1}{S} \cdot \frac{r1 - r2}{r1 r2} = - \frac{Δ1 (r2 - r1)}{S r1 r2}. So α - β is negative, but that's okay.

Thus D = 2k v_x * (α - β) = 2k v_x * ( - \frac{Δ1 (r2 - r1)}{S r1 r2} ) = - \frac{2k v_x Δ1 (r2 - r1)}{S r1 r2}.

Now we have v_x expressed. v_x = \frac{ (r2 - r1) S }{2d}. So plug in:

D = - \frac{2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)}{S r1 r2} * \frac{ (r2 - r1) S }{2d} = - \frac{2k Δ1 (r2 - r1) * (r2 - r1) S}{S r1 r2 * 2d} = - \frac{2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)^2}{2d r1 r2} * (S cancels). So D = - \frac{k Δ1 (r2 - r1)^2}{d r1 r2}.

Thus D = - \frac{k Δ1 (r2 - r1)^2}{d r1 r2}. That's a scalar. Good.

Now we can compute O:

x_O = \frac{0 * w_y - u_y * C2}{D} = - \frac{u_y C2}{D}.
y_O = \frac{u_x * C2 - 0 * w_x}{D} = \frac{u_x C2}{D}.

Now we also have H. Let's compute H in terms of the coordinates. H = (x_P, y_H). We have x_P = \frac{d + r2 - r1}{2}. And y_H from earlier: y_H = \frac{ (x_P - m)(n - x_P) }{ y_P }? But here we have M = (0,0), N = (d,0), so m=0, n=d. Then the orthocenter H of triangle PMN is (x_P, \frac{ (x_P - 0)(d - x_P) }{ y_P } )? Actually we derived a general formula: H = (x_P, \frac{ (x_P - m)(n - x_P) }{ y_P } ) with m=0,n=d. So H_y = \frac{ x_P (d - x_P) }{ y_P }. Since y_P is negative. So H = (x_P, \frac{ x_P (d - x_P) }{ y_P } ). 

But note that earlier we had a different expression for H_y (with (x_P - m)(n - x_P) / y_P). That's correct. So H_y = \frac{ x_P (d - x_P) }{ y_P }. 

Now we also have v = (v_x, v_y). So we need to compute det(O - H, v) = (x_O - x_P) v_y - (y_O - y_H) v_x. This seems messy, but perhaps we can simplify by noting that O is determined by u and w, and we have expressions for u_x, u_y, w_x, w_y. Maybe we can find that O is collinear with something like (0,1) and (v_x, something), and H is something else. But I'm thinking that maybe there is a more clever analytic solution: Use coordinates where MN is the x-axis, and also place the line AP as something like the x-axis? But AP is not necessarily horizontal. But we can rotate such that AP becomes horizontal, then the line through H parallel to AP is horizontal. That would simplify L (horizontal line). But then the geometry becomes different because we need to maintain the circles' centers. Let's consider that.

We have two intersecting circles with centers M and N. The line MN is the line of centers. The common chord AB is perpendicular to MN. AP is some line through A and P (circumcenter of ACD). We could rotate the whole figure such that AP becomes horizontal, then L is horizontal. That might simplify the determinant condition because then the distance from O to L is just the vertical difference (if L is horizontal). But then we need to compute O and H in that rotated coordinate. However, the rotation is just a transformation, and the property we need to prove is invariant under rotations. So we can choose a coordinate system where AP is horizontal. That seems promising.

Let’s define a coordinate system where the line AP is horizontal (parallel to the x-axis). Then L, being parallel to AP, is also horizontal. So L is a horizontal line passing through H. Then the distance from O to L is simply |y_O - y_H|. The radius R is |O - B|. So we need to prove |y_O - y_H| = R. That seems maybe easier? But we also need to compute the coordinates of A, B, M, N, P, etc., in this coordinate system. However, we have the freedom to set up coordinates such that AP is horizontal. That's a rotation of the whole plane. That's allowed. So we can assume without loss of generality that AP is horizontal. Then we can place A and P conveniently.

But we also need to maintain that M and N are on the line MN, which is the line of centers. Since AP is horizontal, that doesn't conflict. However, we need to express the condition that A is an intersection of circles centered at M and N. That's a relation between coordinates. We can choose coordinates such that A = (0,0) or something? But we can also set A as origin. But then AP is horizontal, so P has some x-coordinate and y=0 (since AP horizontal). But we need to incorporate that P is circumcenter of ACD, and A, C, D are known points relative to M and N. That seems messy.

Alternatively, we can choose a coordinate system where MN is horizontal and AP is vertical? That would make L vertical. Then distance from O to L is horizontal difference. That's also simpler. But AP is not necessarily vertical. However, we can rotate such that AP becomes vertical, then L is vertical. That simplifies the distance because then the distance from O to L is just |x_O - x_H|. So we can set AP vertical. That might be easier. Let's try that.

Set AP vertical. Then L is vertical line through H. So L is x = x_H. Then the distance from O to L is |x_O - x_H|. The radius R is |O - B|. So we need to prove |x_O - x_H| = R. That seems perhaps simpler.

But we still need to compute coordinates of points relative to that orientation. However, we can choose a coordinate system where AP is vertical. That's a rotation. So we can assume without loss of generality that AP is vertical. Then we can set A and P on a vertical line. That's convenient.

Now we also have M and N on the line MN. Since AP is vertical, that's okay. But we need to incorporate the circles. Maybe we can place A at origin and AP vertical, so A = (0,0). Then P is on the vertical line (x=0) at some y-coordinate (since AP vertical). So P = (0, p) with p ≠ 0. Then L is the line through H parallel to AP (vertical), so L is vertical line x = x_H. So L is x = x_H. That's a vertical line. The distance from O to L is |x_O - x_H|. That's simple. So we need to show that |x_O - x_H| = R. So we need to compute x_O and x_H. That seems plausible.

Now we need to express everything in coordinates with A = (0,0). But then A is a point on both circles Ω and Γ. That's fine. Then B is the reflection of A across MN (since MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB). But we don't know MN's orientation. However, we have freedom to set up coordinate system such that AP is vertical. That's a rotation. Then we also have the line MN (centers line). But we can also translate such that A is at origin. That's a translation. So we can assume A = (0,0). Then AP is vertical, so P lies somewhere on the y-axis (x=0). Then the line AP is the y-axis. So L is vertical line through H (since parallel to AP). So L is x = x_H. So we need to compute x_H. H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. M and N are points on the line MN. Since AP is vertical, that doesn't fix MN orientation. However, we also know that A is an intersection of circles with centers M and N. So M and N are points such that MA = r1, NA = r2. With A at origin, that gives |M| = r1, |N| = r2. Also, the line MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB. Since A is at origin, B is some point such that the midpoint of AB lies on MN. That's a bit messy.

But maybe we can use the coordinate system where M = (0,0) and N = (d,0) as before, and then rotate such that AP becomes vertical. That rotation would change the coordinates of A, B, etc. But then we can compute expressions. However, we already have a nice expression for |v|^2 and a,b, etc., in the M=(0,0) coordinate. That coordinate seems to have given simplifications. Perhaps we can continue with that coordinate and then compute O and H, and then the determinant. I'm trying to see if the determinant simplifies nicely.

Let's compute O in that coordinate, and also H, and then compute det(O - H, v). We have expressions for u, w, D, C2, and also v_x, v_y. We'll need to compute x_O and y_O in terms of k and v_x, v_y, etc. Then compute O - H. The vector v is known. Then det. That seems heavy but maybe many terms cancel.

Let's try to compute O explicitly using the formulas we derived:

x_O = - (u_y C2) / D
y_O = (u_x C2) / D

where u = (α v_x, 2k + α v_y), with α = a. And D = 2k v_x (α - β) with β = b. So:

x_O = - ( (2k + α v_y) C2 ) / ( 2k v_x (α - β) )
y_O = ( α v_x C2 ) / ( 2k v_x (α - β) ) = ( α C2 ) / ( 2k (α - β) ).

Thus y_O simplifies nicely: y_O = \frac{α C2}{2k (α - β)}.

Now α - β = a - b = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S} - \frac{Δ1}{r1 S} = \frac{Δ1}{S} \cdot \frac{r1 - r2}{r1 r2} = - \frac{Δ1 (r2 - r1)}{S r1 r2}.

So:

α - β = - \frac{Δ1 (r2 - r1)}{S r1 r2}.

Then:

y_O = \frac{α C2}{2k} * \frac{1}{α - β} = \frac{α C2}{2k} * \left( - \frac{S r1 r2}{Δ1 (r2 - r1)} \right) = - \frac{α C2 S r1 r2}{2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)}.

Now α = a = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S}. So α C2 = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S} * C2. Then:

y_O = - \frac{ (Δ1 C2)/(r2 S) * S r1 r2 }{2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)} = - \frac{ (Δ1 C2) * S r1 r2 }{ r2 S * 2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)}? Wait careful:

α C2 = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S} * C2. Then y_O = - \frac{α C2 S r1 r2}{2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)} = - \frac{ \frac{Δ1 C2}{r2 S} * S r1 r2 }{2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)} = - \frac{ Δ1 C2 * S r1 r2 }{ r2 S * 2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)} = - \frac{ Δ1 C2 * r1 }{ 2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)}? Because S cancels, r2 cancels partly? Actually numerator: Δ1 C2 / (r2 S) * S r1 r2 = Δ1 C2 * (S r1 r2) / (r2 S) = Δ1 C2 * r1. So indeed:

α C2 S r1 r2 / (r2 S) = α C2 * r1 = (Δ1/(r2 S)) * C2 * r1 = Δ1 C2 r1/(r2 S). Then we have denominator 2k Δ1 (r2 - r1). So:

y_O = - \frac{ Δ1 C2 r1/(r2 S) }{ 2k Δ1 (r2 - r1) } = - \frac{ C2 r1 }{ 2k (r2 - r1) S }. That's messy. Let's recompute more systematically.

We have y_O = (α C2) / (2k (α - β)). That's from earlier: y_O = \frac{α C2}{2k (α - β)}? Wait we derived:

y_O = \frac{α C2}{2k (α - β)}? Actually from formula: y_O = (u_x C2)/D. u_x = α v_x. D = 2k v_x (α - β). So y_O = (α v_x C2) / (2k v_x (α - β)) = \frac{α C2}{2k (α - β)}. That's correct. So y_O = \frac{α C2}{2k (α - β)}.

We might also compute P using perpendicular bisector of AD. Might be simpler? Let's see.

Now compute (h + r1) and (d + r2 - h). There might be relation between (h + r1) and something like (d + r2 - h) after simplification using the expressions for h. Let's compute h explicitly.

h = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d).

Then compute (d + r2 - h). Substitute h:

d + r2 - h = d + r2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/(2d) = (2d^2 + 2d r2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)) / (2d) = (2d^2 + 2d r2 - d^2 - r1^2 + r2^2) / (2d) = (d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2) / (2d).

That's not obviously simplifying with (h + r1). Compute (h + r1):

h + r1 = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/(2d) + r1 = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2 + 2d r1) / (2d) = (d^2 + 2d r1 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d). So that's ( (d+r1)^2 - r2^2 )/(2d). Similarly, d + r2 - h = (d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2) / (2d). That is something like ( (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 )/(2d)? Let's check: (d + r2)^2 = d^2 + 2d r2 + r2^2. Then (d+r2)^2 - r1^2 = d^2 + 2d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2. That's exactly the numerator we have. So indeed:

d + r2 - h = ((d + r2)^2 - r1^2) / (2d).

However, we might also compute y_P in a more clever way: Since P lies on perpendicular bisector of CD, and also on perpendicular bisector of AC. Perhaps we can compute P by solving the system of equations for the circumcenter of ACD directly using coordinates. Let's denote P = (x, y). Then distances:

|P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2 = |P - D|^2.

So we have:

(1) (x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = (x + r1)^2 + y^2.   (since C = (-r1,0))
(2) (x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = (x - (d + r2))^2 + y^2.

From (1) expand:

(x^2 - 2hx + h^2) + (y^2 - 2ky + k^2) = (x^2 + 2r1 x + r1^2) + y^2.

Cancel x^2 + y^2:

-2hx + h^2 - 2ky + k^2 = 2r1 x + r1^2.

Bring terms:

-2hx - 2r1 x - 2ky + (h^2 + k^2 - r1^2) = 0.

But h^2 + k^2 = r1^2, so h^2 + k^2 - r1^2 = 0. So we have:

-2(h + r1) x - 2k y = 0 => divide -2: (h + r1) x + k y = 0.   (Eq A)

Similarly, from (2):

(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = (x - (d + r2))^2 + y^2.

Expand:

x^2 - 2hx + h^2 + y^2 - 2ky + k^2 = x^2 - 2(d + r2) x + (d + r2)^2 + y^2.

Cancel x^2 + y^2:

-2hx + h^2 - 2ky + k^2 = -2(d + r2) x + (d + r2)^2.

Bring terms:

-2hx + 2(d + r2) x - 2ky + (h^2 + k^2 - (d + r2)^2) = 0.

Now h^2 + k^2 = r1^2. So:

-2hx + 2(d + r2) x - 2ky + (r1^2 - (d + r2)^2) = 0.

Combine x terms: 2( (d + r2) - h) x - 2k y + (r1^2 - (d + r2)^2) = 0.

Divide 2: ((d + r2) - h) x - k y + (r1^2 - (d + r2)^2)/2 = 0.

So we have:

((d + r2) - h) x - k y + (r1^2 - (d + r2)^2)/2 = 0.   (Eq B)

Now we also have the perpendicular bisector of CD (vertical) is x = (d + r2 - r1)/2. That's another equation. But we derived Eq A and Eq B from equalities. Let v = P - A. Then |A + t v|^2 = r1^2.

Expand: |A|^2 + 2 t A·v + t^2 |v|^2 = r1^2. Since |A|^2 = r1^2, we get:

2 t A·v + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 => t (2 A·v + t |v|^2) = 0. So t = 0 (A) or t = -2 A·v / |v|^2. So t_E = -2 A·v / |v|^2.

Similarly, for Γ: Let w = P - A. Then point F is A + t_F * w, with t_F ≠ 0. Condition: |(A + t w) - N| = r2. Since A is on Γ, we have |A - N| = r2. So we have:

2 t (A - N)·w + t^2 |w|^2 = 0 => t (2 (A - N)·w + t |w|^2) = 0. So t_F = -2 (A - N)·w / |w|^2.

Thus we have E and F expressed.

MA^2 = x_M^2 + y0^2 = r1^2  →  x_M^2 = r1^2 - y0^2.
NA^2 = x_N^2 + y0^2 = r2^2  →  x_N^2 = r2^2 - y0^2.

Thus |x_M| < r1, |x_N| < r2. Also, d = MN = |x_N - x_M|.

Now also the problem says "such that the radius of Ω is less than the radius of Γ". So r1 < r2. That's all.

Not necessarily. But we can compute P. The perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line at midpoint of C and D: x = ( (x_M - r1) + (x_N + r2) ) / 2 = ( (x_M + x_N) + (r2 - r1) ) / 2. The perpendicular bisector of AC is something. So we can compute P.

Now this coordinate seems perhaps simpler because A is at (0,y0), so AP direction is easier. Also, B is (0,-y0). So the triangle BEF is symmetric? B is on y-axis, E and F are on AP (which may be some line). That's okay.

But we still have many parameters: r1, r2, d (or x_M,x_N). But we have relation between x_M, x_N, y0, r1, r2. So we can reduce to two independent variables, e.g., r1 and r2 and distance d (or maybe y0 and d). Since A and B are intersection points, we can compute y0 and x_M,x_N from d,r1,r2. Let's derive.

Given M = (x_M,0), N = (x_N,0), with x_M < x_N. Then:

MA^2 = x_M^2 + y0^2 = r1^2  (1)
NA^2 = (x_N)^2 + y0^2 = r2^2  (2)
MN = d = x_N - x_M > 0.

We can solve for x_M and x_N. Subtract (2)-(1): x_N^2 - x_M^2 = r2^2 - r1^2. So (x_N - x_M)(x_N + x_M) = r2^2 - r1^2. Let S = x_N + x_M. Then d * S = r2^2 - r1^2. So S = (r2^2 - r1^2) / d.

Then we also have (x_N - x_M)^2 = d^2. And we have x_M^2 + y0^2 = r1^2. So we can find x_M and x_N individually:

x_M = (S - d)/2 = ( (r2^2 - r1^2)/d - d ) / 2 = ( (r2^2 - r1^2) - d^2 ) / (2d).
x_N = (S + d)/2 = ( (r2^2 - r1^2)/d + d ) / 2 = ( (r2^2 - r1^2) + d^2 ) / (2d).

Thus we get expressions similar to earlier but with signs. Previously we had M = (0,0), N = (d,0) and A = (h,k) with h = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/(2d). That's essentially the same if we translate. So both coordinate systems are equivalent.

Now, I'll choose a coordinate system that seems to simplify P. Since A is at (0,y0) and C and D are on x-axis, then P's x-coordinate is something like ( (x_M - r1) + (x_N + r2) ) / 2. That's a bit messy but maybe simplifies. Let's compute with the new coordinates.

The perpendicular bisector of AC (or AD). Let's use AC.

A = (0,a), C = (m - r1, 0). Midpoint: ((m - r1)/2, a/2). Slope of AC: (0 - a) / (m - r1 - 0) = -a / (m - r1). The perpendicular slope is (m - r1) / a. So equation:

(y - a/2) = ( (m - r1) / a ) * (x - (m - r1)/2).

Then P's x-coordinate is as above. So we can compute y_P.

Alternatively, use the distance equalities to derive linear equations. That might be easier. Since we have coordinates, we can compute directly.

Let’s denote:

x_P = ( (m - r1) + (n + r2) ) / 2 = (m + n + r2 - r1) / 2.

Now compute y_P from |P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2. That yields:

(x_P)^2 + (y_P - a)^2 = (x_P - (m - r1))^2 + (y_P - 0)^2.

Expand:

x_P^2 + y_P^2 - 2a y_P + a^2 = (x_P - (m - r1))^2 + y_P^2.

Cancel y_P^2. So:

x_P^2 - 2a y_P + a^2 = x_P^2 - 2x_P (m - r1) + (m - r1)^2.

Cancel x_P^2:

-2a y_P + a^2 = -2x_P (m - r1) + (m - r1)^2.

Thus:

-2a y_P = -2x_P (m - r1) + (m - r1)^2 - a^2.

Multiply by -1: 2a y_P = 2x_P (m - r1) - (m - r1)^2 + a^2.

So:

y_P = [ 2x_P (m - r1) - (m - r1)^2 + a^2 ] / (2a).

Now we also have relation: a^2 = r1^2 - m^2. Because MA^2 = m^2 + a^2 = r1^2. So a^2 = r1^2 - m^2. Similarly, a^2 = r2^2 - n^2. So that's consistent.

(A - N)·v = (h - d)x_P + d h - (h + r1)x_P - r1^2.

Combine x_P terms: (h - d - h - r1) x_P = (-d - r1) x_P. So:

(A - N)·v = (-d - r1) x_P + d h - r1^2.

Thus (A - N)·v = - (d + r1) x_P + d h - r1^2.

Now we can also express d h in terms of h and d. Since h = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/(2d), then d h = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/2. So we can plug that. Then:

(A - N)·v = - (d + r1) x_P + (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/2 - r1^2.

Simplify the constant part: (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/2 - r1^2 = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2 - 2 r1^2)/2 = (d^2 - r1^2 - r2^2)/2.

Thus:

(A - N)·v = - (d + r1) x_P + (d^2 - r1^2 - r2^2)/2.

Now we also need x_P expressed in terms of d,r1,r2. x_P = (d + r2 - r1)/2.

So we can compute - (d + r1) x_P = - (d + r1) * (d + r2 - r1)/2.

Let's compute that:

(d + r1)(d + r2 - r1) = (d + r1)(d + r2 - r1). Expand: (d)(d + r2 - r1) + r1(d + r2 - r1) = d^2 + d r2 - d r1 + r1 d + r1 r2 - r1^2 = d^2 + d r2 - d r1 + d r1 + r1 r2 - r1^2 = d^2 + d r2 + r1 r2 - r1^2. So:

- (d + r1) x_P = - [ (d^2 + d r2 + r1 r2 - r1^2) ] / 2.

Now compute (d^2 - r1^2 - r2^2)/2. So sum:

(A - N)·v = - (d^2 + d r2 + r1 r2 - r1^2)/2 + (d^2 - r1^2 - r2^2)/2.

Combine over denominator 2: 

(A - N)·v = [ - (d^2 + d r2 + r1 r2 - r1^2) + (d^2 - r1^2 - r2^2) ] / 2.

Simplify numerator: 

- d^2 - d r2 - r1 r2 + r1^2 + d^2 - r1^2 - r2^2 = (-d^2 + d^2) + (- d r2) + (- r1 r2) + (r1^2 - r1^2) - r2^2 = - d r2 - r1 r2 - r2^2.

Thus (A - N)·v = - ( d r2 + r1 r2 + r2^2 ) / 2 = - r2 ( d + r1 + r2 ) / 2.

So we get:

(A - N)·v = - r2 (d + r1 + r2) / 2.

That is a neat simplification. Let's double-check with numeric example: r1=3, r2=5, d=4 → (A - N)·v = -5 * (4+3+5)/2 = -5 * 12/2 = -5 * 6 = -30. Earlier we computed (A - N)·v = -30, matches. So good.

Thus we have:

A·v = - r1 (x_P + r1). Since x_P = (d + r2 - r1)/2, we can also simplify x_P + r1 = (d + r2 - r1)/2 + r1 = (d + r2 - r1 + 2 r1)/2 = (d + r2 + r1)/2. So:

A·v = - r1 * ( (d + r1 + r2) / 2 ) = - r1 (d + r1 + r2) / 2.

Similarly, (A - N)·v = - r2 (d + r1 + r2) / 2.

Actually if we have two parallel lines L1 and L2, then for any point O, the signed distance to L2 is signed distance to L1 plus (signed distance between the lines) depending on orientation. Let’s denote:

Eq1: (a v_x) x + (2k + a v_y) y = 0.
Eq2: (b v_x) x + (2k + b v_y) y = C2.

We can solve this 2x2 linear system. The determinant D = (a v_x)(2k + b v_y) - (b v_x)(2k + a v_y) = v_x [ a (2k + b v_y) - b (2k + a v_y) ] = v_x [ 2k a + a b v_y - 2k b - a b v_y ] = v_x * 2k (a - b). So D = 2k v_x (a - b). That's nice.

Thus D is non-zero (assuming v_x ≠ 0, a ≠ b). v_x = ((r2 - r1)S)/(2d) is non-zero because r2 > r1. a and b are different because r1 ≠ r2. So D ≠ 0.

Then we can solve for x and y:

x = ( (0)*(2k + b v_y) - (2k + a v_y) * C2 ) / D? Actually using Cramer's rule:

x = det( [0, (2k + a v_y); C2, (2k + b v_y)] ) / D = (0 * (2k + b v_y) - (2k + a v_y) * C2) / D = - C2 (2k + a v_y) / D.

y = det( [a v_x, 0; b v_x, C2] ) / D = (a v_x * C2 - 0 * b v_x) / D = a v_x C2 / D.

Thus:

x = - C2 (2k + a v_y) / (2k v_x (a - b)).
y = a v_x C2 / (2k v_x (a - b)) = a C2 / (2k (a - b)).

So y is simplified: y = a C2 / (2k (a - b)). And x simplifies as well.

Now note that a - b = (r1 S - r2 S) / |v|^2 = S (r1 - r2) / |v|^2 = - S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2. So a - b is negative. That's okay.

Then C2 = - (r1 S b) / 2 + (b^2 |v|^2) / 2 = (1/2) ( b^2 |v|^2 - r1 S b ).

Now plug b = (r2 S) / |v|^2. Then b^2 |v|^2 = (r2^2 S^2) / |v|^4 * |v|^2 = r2^2 S^2 / |v|^2. Also, r1 S b = r1 S * (r2 S / |v|^2) = r1 r2 S^2 / |v|^2. So:

C2 = (1/2) ( (r2^2 S^2 / |v|^2) - (r1 r2 S^2 / |v|^2) ) = (1/2) * (S^2 r2 (r2 - r1) / |v|^2) = (S^2 r2 (r2 - r1)) / (2 |v|^2). This matches earlier expression with r2(r2 - r1) (since r2 - r1 > 0). So C2 = (S^2 r2 (r2 - r1)) / (2 |v|^2). Good.

Now a = (r1 S) / |v|^2. So a - b = S (r1 - r2) / |v|^2 = - S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2.

Thus (a - b) = - S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2.

Now compute y = a C2 / (2k (a - b)). Substitute:

a C2 = (r1 S / |v|^2) * (S^2 r2 (r2 - r1) / (2 |v|^2)) = r1 r2 S^3 (r2 - r1) / (2 |v|^4). Then divide by (2k (a - b)). So:

y = [ r1 r2 S^3 (r2 - r1) / (2 |v|^4) ] / [ 2k * ( - S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2 ) ] = [ r1 r2 S^3 (r2 - r1) / (2 |v|^4) ] * [ 1 / (2k * (- S (r2 - r1) / |v|^2) ) ] = [ r1 r2 S^3 (r2 - r1) / (2 |v|^4) ] * [ |v|^2 / (2k * (- S (r2 - r1)) ] = [ r1 r2 S^3 (r2 - r1) * |v|^2 ] / [ 2 |v|^4 * 2k * (- S (r2 - r1)) ] = [ r1 r2 S^3 (r2 - r1) |v|^2 ] / [ -4 k |v|^4 S (r2 - r1) ].

Cancel (r2 - r1) (assuming non-zero). Cancel one S: S^3 / S = S^2. So:

y = [ r1 r2 S^2 |v|^2 ] / [ -4 k |v|^4 ] = [ r1 r2 S^2 ] / [ -4 k |v|^2 ] = - (r1 r2 S^2) / (4 k |v|^2).

Thus y = - (r1 r2 S^2) / (4 k |v|^2). That's a nice expression.

Now compute x = - C2 (2k + a v_y) / (2k v_x (a - b)). But we might also find a simpler expression for O. Note that y is independent of v_x, v_y. So O's y-coordinate is determined. Now we can also compute O's x-coordinate. But perhaps we can find a more elegant approach: The circumcenter O of BEF can be expressed as the midpoint of the segment connecting the orthocenters of something? Not.

Alternatively, we can compute O by solving the linear system using Cramer's rule, but we already got y. We also need to compute det(O - H, v) and R|v|. Since we have expressions for O and H, we can compute. But we also need to compute R = |O - B|. That seems messy.

Maybe we can prove tangency by showing that H is the orthocenter of triangle PMN, and that line through H parallel to AP is the polar of A with respect to the circle (BEF), which would be tangent if A lies on the circle. But A does not lie on that circle, so not.

Wait, perhaps we misinterpreted the problem: "Prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF." That's what we have. So we need to show that the line L (through H parallel to AP) touches the circle (BEF). That is, L is a tangent line to that circle. Since L passes through H, but H is not necessarily on the circle. So we need to show that L is tangent, meaning that it intersects the circle at exactly one point. That point is the point of tangency. So L is not necessarily passing through a vertex of the triangle BEF, it's just a line.

So we need to prove that distance from center O to L equals radius. That's a standard condition. So if we can compute O and then compute the distance, we can verify equality. The challenge is to compute O in a manageable way. Perhaps we can find that O lies on the line through M (origin) perpendicular to something, and that O's coordinates can be expressed nicely using a and b and the parameters. Let's try to compute O directly using the formula for the circumcenter in terms of coordinates. There's a known formula: For three points P1,P2,P3, the circumcenter is given by:

O = ( \frac{ |P1|^2 (P2 - P3) + |P2|^2 (P3 - P1) + |P3|^2 (P1 - P2) }{ 2 (P1 × P2 + P2 × P3 + P3 × P1) } )? That's for complex numbers. In vector form, it's easier to solve the linear system.

We have B, E, F. B = (h,-k). E = A + a v, F = A + b v. So we can compute O by solving:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 → O·(E - B) = (|E|^2 - |B|^2)/2.
|O - B|^2 = |O - F|^2 → O·(F - B) = (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2.

That's two linear equations. So we can solve for O. We already did that and got expressions for x and y. But we need to also incorporate the fact that O is the circumcenter, so it's uniquely determined. That's fine. Now we can compute O - H and then det(O - H, v). That seems doable. Let's continue with that approach.

We have M = (0,0). So coordinates.

w = F - B = (b v_x, 2k + b v_y)

But earlier we simplified to y_O = - (r1 r2 S^2) / (4 k |v|^2). That seems correct after simplification. Let's re-evaluate that simplification carefully.

We had:

= \frac{1}{2} \left( r2 - r1 - \frac{r1^2 - r2^2}{d} \right) = \frac{1}{2} \left( r2 - r1 - \frac{(r1 - r2)(r1 + r2)}{d} \right) = \frac{1}{2} \left( r2 - r1 + \frac{(r2 - r1)(r1 + r2)}{d} \right) (since r1^2 - r2^2 = (r1 - r2)(r1 + r2) = -(r2 - r1)(r1 + r2)). So:

x_P - h = \frac{1}{2} \left( (r2 - r1) + \frac{(r2 - r1)(r1 + r2)}{d} \right) = \frac{1}{2} (r2 - r1) \left( 1 + \frac{r1 + r2}{d} \right) = \frac{(r2 - r1)}{2} \cdot \frac{d + r1 + r2}{d} = \frac{(r2 - r1) S}{2d}.

Thus:

|v|^2 = \frac{4 r1 r2 ( r1 + h + d/2 ) ( r2 - h + d/2 )}{4 k^2} = \frac{r1 r2 ( r1 + h + d/2 ) ( r2 - h + d/2 )}{k^2}.

Now note that ( r1 + h + d/2 ) and ( r2 - h + d/2 ) are related. We have h expressed. Let's compute these expressions in terms of d, r1, r2. We have h = (r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d). Then:

r1 + h + d/2 = r1 + \frac{r1^2 - r2^2}{2d} + \frac{d}{2}. Combine over denominator 2d: \frac{2 r1 d + r1^2 - r2^2 + d^2}{2d} = \frac{ (d^2 + 2 r1 d + r1^2) - r2^2 }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r1)^2 - r2^2 }{2d}. That's something like ( (d+r1)^2 - r2^2 )/(2d). Similarly, r2 - h + d/2 = r2 - \frac{r1^2 - r2^2}{2d} + \frac{d}{2} = \frac{2 r2 d - (r1^2 - r2^2) + d^2}{2d} = \frac{2 r2 d - r1^2 + r2^2 + d^2}{2d} = \frac{ (d^2 + 2 r2 d + r2^2) - r1^2 }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 }{2d}. So:

(r1 + h + d/2) = \frac{ (d + r1)^2 - r2^2 }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r1 - r2)(d + r1 + r2) }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r1 - r2) S }{2d}.
(r2 - h + d/2) = \frac{ (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r2 - r1)(d + r2 + r1) }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r2 - r1) S }{2d}.

Thus:

(r1 + h + d/2) (r2 - h + d/2) = \frac{ S^2 (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) }{4 d^2}.

Now note that (d + r1 - r2) and (d + r2 - r1) are both positive? Since d > |r2 - r1|, we have d > r2 - r1, so d + r1 - r2 > 0. Similarly, d + r2 - r1 > 0. So they are positive.

Thus:

|v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2}{k^2} * \frac{ S^2 (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) }{4 d^2}.

Now also k^2 can be expressed as ((r1 + r2)^2 - d^2)(d^2 - (r2 - r1)^2)) / (4 d^2). That's a bit messy. But maybe we can simplify |v|^2 to something like ( (r1 r2) S^2 ) / (something). Let's compute |v|^2 in terms of k and other parameters.

Now recall that a = r1 S / |v|^2. So a = r1 S / ( \frac{r1 r2}{k^2} * \frac{ S^2 (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) }{4 d^2} ) = r1 S * \frac{ k^2 * 4 d^2 }{ r1 r2 S^2 (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) } = \frac{ 4 d^2 k^2 }{ r2 S (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) }.

That's a expression for a. Similarly, b = r2 S / |v|^2 = \frac{ 4 d^2 k^2 }{ r1 S (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) }.

Now also note that (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) = (d^2 - (r2 - r1)^2) maybe? Let's compute: (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) = (d + (r1 - r2)) (d - (r1 - r2)) = d^2 - (r1 - r2)^2 = d^2 - (r2 - r1)^2. So indeed that's Δ2, the factor in k^2. And (r1 + r2)^2 - d^2 is Δ1. So we have:

k^2 = \frac{ \Delta1 \Delta2 }{4 d^2}. So that's consistent.

Now we have expressions for a and b. That seems promising. Now we can compute v_x and v_y. v_x = x_P - h. We already have v_x = \frac{ (r2 - r1) S }{2 d}. Also, we can compute v_y. But perhaps we can find a simpler expression for (2k + a v_y). Let's try to compute v_y using the relation A·v. But maybe we can find an expression for v_y in terms of k and other knowns from the formula for y_P. Since we have |v|^2 expressed, and we also have the expression for y_P from earlier, we could compute y_P - k directly. That might be messy. However, note that we have a nice expression for a and b, and also we have C2 = (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2 = \frac{S^2 r2 (r2 - r1)}{2 |v|^2}. That's simple.

Actually AD^2 is always positive, so (r2 + n) must be positive because r2 > 0. If n is negative enough such that r2 + n < 0, then 2 r2 (r2 + n) would be negative, contradiction. Therefore r2 + n > 0. So n > -r2. Since |n| ≤ r2, that's okay. So n could be negative, but must be > -r2. So r2 + n > 0. That's consistent with the condition that the distance from A to N is r2, and the foot is (0,0). So n could be negative, but not less than -r2. So n ∈ (-r2, r2]. Similarly, m ∈ (-r1, r1]. That's fine.

(r1 - m) = \frac{a^2}{r1 + m} (since (r1 - m)(r1 + m) = a^2).

Now substitute b = r2 S / |v|^2. Then:

b^2 |v|^2 = (r2^2 S^2 / |v|^4) * |v|^2 = r2^2 S^2 / |v|^2.
b r1 S = (r2 S / |v|^2) * r1 S = r1 r2 S^2 / |v|^2.

Thus C2 = ( - r1 r2 S^2 / |v|^2 + r2^2 S^2 / |v|^2 ) / 2 = \frac{S^2 r2 (r2 - r1)}{2 |v|^2}.

That's consistent.

Now we also need v_x and v_y. v = P - A. P is circumcenter of ACD. Let's compute P explicitly in this coordinate. We have:

Thus v = (x_P - h, y_P - k).

Now compute v_x = (d + r2 - r1)/2 - h. We derived earlier v_x = ((r2 - r1)S) / (2d). That's a nice expression. Let's confirm:

That seems heavy but perhaps we can find a simplification: Since we have expressions for a, b, and |v|^2, maybe we can compute O in terms of A, v, and the parameters. Let's try to solve the linear system in a vector form.

We have B, E, F. Let’s denote vectors in ℝ^2. We can write O as unknown.

Equations:

(1) |O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 → (O - B)·(O - B) = (O - E)·(O - E) → -2 O·B + |B|^2 = -2 O·E + |E|^2 → 2 O·(E - B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2.

(2) |O - B|^2 = |O - F|^2 → 2 O·(F - B) = |F|^2 - |B|^2.

Now |E|^2 - |B|^2 = 2 b (A·v) + b^2 |v|^2, because E = A + b v. Similarly, |F|^2 - |B|^2 = 2 a (A·v) + a^2 |v|^2, but careful: F = A + a v? Actually we defined a = t_F (the parameter for F) and b = t_E (the parameter for E). But earlier we had E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v. But we need to be consistent: In the M=(0,0) coordinate, we had E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v. Then a = t_F (for F) and b = t_E (for E). But in our earlier formulas, we used a = r1 S / |v|^2 (for E) and b = r2 S / |v|^2 (for F). That's fine. But then the RHS for equation with F (which we used for C2) is (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2. That's C2 = (2 a (A·v) + a^2 |v|^2)/2? Actually |F|^2 - |B|^2 = 2 a (A·v) + a^2 |v|^2. That's if we set a = t_F (the parameter for F). So we need to define:

Let α = t_E (for E) and β = t_F (for F). But we earlier derived a = r1 S / |v|^2, b = r2 S / |v|^2. Which is which? We computed E and F using param with v = P - A. We had:

E = A + t_E v, with t_E = -2 (A·v) / |v|^2. Since A·v = - r1 S / 2, then t_E = -2 * (- r1 S / 2) / |v|^2 = (r1 S) / |v|^2. So t_E = r1 S / |v|^2.

Similarly, t_F = -2 (A - N)·v / |v|^2. Since (A - N)·v = - r2 S / 2, then t_F = -2 * (- r2 S / 2) / |v|^2 = (r2 S) / |v|^2. So t_F = r2 S / |v|^2. So indeed E corresponds to parameter r1 S / |v|^2, F corresponds to r2 S / |v|^2. So we have:

E = A + (r1 S / |v|^2) v.
F = A + (r2 S / |v|^2) v.

Thus we can denote:

u1 = r1 S / |v|^2 (for E), u2 = r2 S / |v|^2 (for F). So the larger is u2 (since r2 > r1). That's fine.

Now compute |E|^2 - |B|^2 = 2 u1 (A·v) + u1^2 |v|^2. Since A·v = - r1 S / 2, then:

2 u1 (A·v) = 2 * (r1 S / |v|^2) * (- r1 S / 2) = - r1^2 S^2 / |v|^2.
u1^2 |v|^2 = (r1^2 S^2 / |v|^4) * |v|^2 = r1^2 S^2 / |v|^2.
So |E|^2 - |B|^2 = - r1^2 S^2 / |v|^2 + r1^2 S^2 / |v|^2 = 0. So indeed the RHS for equation involving E is 0. That's a crucial simplification: O·(E - B) = 0. So O is orthogonal to (E - B). This is independent of coordinate. So that's a linear equation.

Now for F: |F|^2 - |B|^2 = 2 u2 (A·v) + u2^2 |v|^2. u2 = r2 S / |v|^2. Then 2 u2 (A·v) = 2 * (r2 S / |v|^2) * (- r1 S / 2) = - r1 r2 S^2 / |v|^2. u2^2 |v|^2 = (r2^2 S^2 / |v|^4) * |v|^2 = r2^2 S^2 / |v|^2. So:

|F|^2 - |B|^2 = (r2^2 - r1 r2) S^2 / |v|^2 = r2 (r2 - r1) S^2 / |v|^2. Thus C2 = (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2 = \frac{r2 (r2 - r1) S^2}{2 |v|^2}.

Now also note that (E - B) = (A - B) + u1 v. But we already have that.

Now we have O·u = 0, where u = E - B. And O·w = C2, where w = F - B.

Now we can solve for O. That's what we did. The expression for y_O turned out to be something like - (r1 r2 S^2) / (4 k |v|^2). That seems correct. Let's derive again in a general coordinate with A = (h,k). But we need to confirm that the expression for y_O (the y-coordinate) is independent of the coordinate choice? Actually we solved the linear system with coordinates, so it's correct.

Now we need to compute det(O - H, v). Maybe we can find a relationship between O and H such that O - H is parallel to something like (k, something) and v is something, making the determinant equal to R|v|. Perhaps we can compute O explicitly by solving the system in terms of the vectors u and w. Since we have O·u = 0 and O·w = C2. This is a system of two linear equations in O (if we treat O as a vector). However, note that O is a point (position vector). The equations are dot products with vectors u and w. If u and w are linearly independent, we can solve for O as a linear combination of u and w. But we need to solve for O. Write O = α u + β w? Actually then O·u = α |u|^2 + β (u·w). That's not directly α and β. So better to treat as linear equations: u^T O = 0, w^T O = C2. This is a 2x2 linear system. The solution O is given by:

O = \frac{1}{det} \begin{pmatrix} 0 & -C2 \\ C2 & 0 \end{pmatrix}? Actually we can solve using Cramer's rule. The determinant of the coefficient matrix is det([u; w]) = u_x w_y - u_y w_x. So O = (0 * w_perp - C2 * u_perp?) Actually we need to solve:

u_x x + u_y y = 0.
w_x x + w_y y = C2.

Then solution:

x = \frac{0 * w_y - u_y * C2}{u_x w_y - u_y w_x} = - \frac{u_y C2}{D},
y = \frac{u_x * C2 - 0 * w_x}{D} = \frac{u_x C2}{D},

where D = u_x w_y - u_y w_x = det([u; w]). That's a nice formula. So we can compute O in terms of u and w. Then O - H can be expressed.

Now H is (x_P, y_H). We have expressions for u and w. Let's compute u = E - B and w = F - B. But perhaps we can simplify by noting that u is proportional to something like (v_x, something) and w is proportional to (v_x, something). Let's compute u and w explicitly in terms of v and A - B. We have:

A - B = (0, 2k) if A = (h,k), B = (h,-k). So A - B is vertical. So:

u = (A - B) + u1 v = (0, 2k) + u1 v. Similarly, w = (0, 2k) + u2 v.

But careful: E = A + u1 v, so E - B = (A - B) + u1 v. That's correct. So u = (0, 2k) + u1 v. w = (0, 2k) + u2 v.

Now v is P - A. We know v_x and v_y.

Now we can compute D = det(u, w) = det( (0, 2k) + u1 v, (0, 2k) + u2 v ). Since determinant is bilinear and alternating, we can expand:

det(u, w) = det( (0, 2k), (0, 2k) ) + u1 det( (0, 2k), v ) + u2 det( (0, 2k), v )? Wait careful: det(A + u1 v, B + u2 v) = det(A, B) + u1 det(A, v) + u2 det(B, v) + u1 u2 det(v, v). Since det(v, v) = 0. So:

det(u, w) = det( (0, 2k), (0, 2k) ) + u1 det( (0, 2k), v ) + u2 det( (0, 2k), v )? Actually B = (0, 2k) also. So both A and B are (0, 2k). So:

det(u, w) = det( (0, 2k), (0, 2k) ) + u1 det( (0, 2k), v ) + u2 det( (0, 2k), v ) = 0 + (u1 + u2) det( (0, 2k), v ).

Thus D = (u1 + u2) * det( (0, 2k), v ). That's nice.

Now det( (0, 2k), v ) = (0)*v_y - (2k)*v_x = -2k v_x.

So D = (u1 + u2) * (-2k v_x) = -2k v_x (u1 + u2).

Now u1 + u2 = (r1 S)/|v|^2 + (r2 S)/|v|^2 = S (r1 + r2) / |v|^2. So D = -2k v_x * S (r1 + r2) / |v|^2. That's a scalar. Then we can compute O:

x_O = - (u_y C2) / D? Wait earlier we had x = - (u_y C2) / D, y = (u_x C2) / D. That was for solving u·x = 0, w·x = C2. But we used u_x, u_y as components of u. So we need to compute u_x and u_y.

u = (0, 2k) + u1 v. So u_x = u1 v_x, u_y = 2k + u1 v_y. Similarly, w_x = u2 v_x, w_y = 2k + u2 v_y.

Then D = u_x w_y - u_y w_x. We computed D = (u1 + u2) * (-2k v_x). Let's double-check with this expansion:

det(u,w) = det( (u1 v_x, 2k + u1 v_y), (u2 v_x, 2k + u2 v_y) ). Expand:

u_x w_y - u_y w_x = (u1 v_x)*(2k + u2 v_y) - (2k + u1 v_y)*(u2 v_x) = u1 v_x * 2k + u1 v_x * u2 v_y - 2k * u2 v_x - u1 v_y * u2 v_x = 2k u1 v_x + u1 u2 v_x v_y - 2k u2 v_x - u1 u2 v_x v_y = 2k v_x (u1 - u2). So D = 2k v_x (u1 - u2). That's different from the earlier expansion with (0,2k) + u1 v and (0,2k) + u2 v. Let's compute again carefully:

We have u = (0, 2k) + u1 v = (u1 v_x, 2k + u1 v_y). w = (0, 2k) + u2 v = (u2 v_x, 2k + u2 v_y).

Compute determinant:

| u_x  u_y |
| w_x  w_y | = u_x w_y - u_y w_x.

u_x w_y = (u1 v_x) * (2k + u2 v_y) = 2k u1 v_x + u1 u2 v_x v_y.
u_y w_x = (2k + u1 v_y) * (u2 v_x) = 2k u2 v_x + u1 u2 v_x v_y.

Thus u_x w_y - u_y w_x = 2k u1 v_x + u1 u2 v_x v_y - 2k u2 v_x - u1 u2 v_x v_y = 2k v_x (u1 - u2). So D = 2k v_x (u1 - u2). That's correct. In my earlier expansion I incorrectly assumed that det(A,B) where A = (0,2k), B = (0,2k) is 0, and then added contributions from u1 v and u2 v, but I missed that the terms with u1 v and u2 v also involve the (0,2k) part? Which one is correct? Let's compute numerically with the example to see which formula holds. In the example, M=(0,0), N=(4,0), A=(0,3), B=(0,-3), v = (3,-6). But maybe there's a more clever way: Since we have O·u = 0, and u is proportional to something like (v_x, something). That's one linear equation. The other is O·w = C2.
