Maybe we can find an expression for O - H that is parallel to something like (something) and v is something else, making the determinant equal to R|v|. Let's try to compute O explicitly using the solution from linear equations with u and w.

We have:

u = (u1 v_x, 2k + u1 v_y)
w = (u2 v_x, 2k + u2 v_y)

D = det(u,w) = u1 v_x * (2k + u2 v_y) - (2k + u1 v_y) * u2 v_x = 2k v_x (u1 - u2). As derived.

Now C2 = \frac{r2 (r2 - r1) S^2}{2 |v|^2}. But we can also express C2 in terms of u2 and |v|^2, but that's okay.

Now O:

x_O = \frac{0 * w_y - u_y * C2}{D} = -\frac{u_y C2}{D}
y_O = \frac{u_x * C2 - 0 * w_x}{D} = \frac{u_x C2}{D}

Now we need to compute det(O - H, v). H = (x_P, y_H). And v = (v_x, v_y). So O - H = (x_O - x_P, y_O - y_H).

The determinant det(O - H, v) = (x_O - x_P) v_y - (y_O - y_H) v_x. This seems messy. However, we might be able to prove that L is tangent by showing that the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) is equal to the square of the distance from H to the line L (which is zero), but that doesn't give tangency. Actually we need a condition that L is tangent. Since H lies on L, if we can show that the power of H with respect to (BEF) equals (distance from H to the point of tangency)^2, but we don't know the point. However, we can consider the line L and the circle (BEF). The condition for L to be tangent is that the distance from the center O to L equals R. If we can find an expression for O in terms of H and v such that O - H is perpendicular to v? Not exactly. Let's examine the numeric example: O - H = (-1, -3). v = (3,-6). Dot product: (-1)*3 + (-3)*(-6) = -3 + 18 = 15, not zero. So not perpendicular. But the distance from O to L is |det(O - H, v)| / |v|. So we need that to equal R. That's the relation we need to prove.

Now we have expressions for O and H. Perhaps we can compute det(O - H, v) and then compare with R|v|, using the formulas for u, w, etc. Let's attempt to compute O - H in terms of v, u, w, and the parameters. But we also have H expressed in terms of x_P, y_H. And we have expressions for x_P and y_H from earlier.

Maybe we can find a more synthetic relationship: The line through H parallel to AP is L. Consider the circle with diameter MN. Its center is midpoint of MN. The line AP is a line that is the radical axis of something? Not.

Given the complexity, I think it's best to proceed with an analytic solution using coordinates where M = (0,0) and N = (d,0). Then compute P, v, a, b, C2, then compute O using the linear system. Then compute det(O - H, v). Then compute R^2 = |O - B|^2. Then show equality. That is a lot of algebra, but with careful simplification, it should be doable. I'll try to do it step by step, and see if cancellations occur.

First, define:

M = (0,0)
N = (d,0)
A = (h,k)
B = (h,-k)

We have relations:

(1) h^2 + k^2 = r1^2
(2) (h - d)^2 + k^2 = r2^2

Thus:

h^2 = r1^2 - k^2
(h - d)^2 = r2^2 - k^2

Expand (h - d)^2 = h^2 - 2hd + d^2. So:

h^2 - 2hd + d^2 = r2^2 - k^2

Substitute h^2 = r1^2 - k^2:

r1^2 - k^2 - 2hd + d^2 = r2^2 - k^2 → r1^2 - 2hd + d^2 = r2^2 → 2hd = r1^2 + d^2 - r2^2 → h = (r1^2 + d^2 - r2^2) / (2d). This is the same as before. So we have h expressed.

Now we need P. As before, x_P = (C_x + D_x)/2 = ( -r1 + (d + r2) ) / 2 = (d + r2 - r1)/2.
y_P from |PA|^2 = |PC|^2 → (x_P - h)^2 + (y_P - k)^2 = (x_P + r1)^2 + y_P^2 (since C = (-r1,0)). This yields:

(x_P - h)^2 + y_P^2 - 2k y_P + k^2 = x_P^2 + 2 r1 x_P + r1^2 + y_P^2

Cancel y_P^2 and x_P^2? Actually (x_P - h)^2 = x_P^2 - 2 x_P h + h^2. So:

x_P^2 - 2 x_P h + h^2 - 2k y_P + k^2 = x_P^2 + 2 r1 x_P + r1^2.

Cancel x_P^2:

-2 x_P h + h^2 - 2k y_P + k^2 = 2 r1 x_P + r1^2

Thus:

-2k y_P = 2 r1 x_P + r1^2 + 2 x_P h - h^2 - k^2

But h^2 + k^2 = r1^2, so -h^2 - k^2 = -r1^2. Then:

-2k y_P = 2 r1 x_P + r1^2 + 2 x_P h - r1^2 = 2 r1 x_P + 2 x_P h = 2 x_P (r1 + h). So:

-2k y_P = 2 x_P (r1 + h) → y_P = - \frac{ x_P (r1 + h) }{ k }.

That's the same as earlier. So y_P = - (x_P (r1 + h)) / k. That's a linear relation. Good.

Now compute v = P - A = (x_P - h, y_P - k). So:

v_x = x_P - h.
v_y = y_P - k = - \frac{x_P (r1 + h)}{k} - k.

Now we also have expressions for x_P, h, etc. We can compute v_x in terms of d, r1, r2. We already did: v_x = ((r2 - r1) S) / (2d). That's nice. So:

v_x = \frac{ (r2 - r1) S }{ 2d }.

Now compute (r1 + h). Using h expression:

r1 + h = r1 + \frac{r1^2 + d^2 - r2^2}{2d} = \frac{2 r1 d + r1^2 + d^2 - r2^2}{2d}. That's not as simple. But we can also compute (r1 + h) in terms of S and d? Let's compute S = d + r1 + r2. Then r1 + h = \frac{ (d + r1)^2 - r2^2 }{2d}? Actually earlier we derived that (r1 + h) = ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d). Let's verify:

We have h = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d). Then r1 + h = r1 + (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = (2 r1 d + d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = ( (d^2 + 2 r1 d + r1^2) - r2^2 ) / (2d) = ( (d + r1)^2 - r2^2 ) / (2d). That's correct. So (r1 + h) = \frac{ (d + r1)^2 - r2^2 }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r1 - r2)(d + r1 + r2) }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r1 - r2) S }{2d}. That's positive because d + r1 - r2 > 0. Good.

Thus:

x_P (r1 + h) = \frac{ d + r2 - r1 }{2} * \frac{ (d + r1 - r2) S }{2d} = \frac{ S (d + r2 - r1)(d + r1 - r2) }{4 d}. 

Now (d + r2 - r1)(d + r1 - r2) = (d^2 - (r2 - r1)^2) = Δ2. So:

x_P (r1 + h) = \frac{ S Δ2 }{4 d}.

Then y_P = - \frac{ x_P (r1 + h) }{ k } = - \frac{ S Δ2 }{4 d k }.

Now compute v_y = y_P - k = - \frac{ S Δ2 }{4 d k } - k.

Thus v_y = -k - \frac{ S Δ2 }{4 d k }.

Now we have v_x, v_y, and also a and b (parameters for E and F). Recall:

a = t_F = (r2 S) / |v|^2.
b = t_E = (r1 S) / |v|^2.

We also need |v|^2. Let's compute |v|^2 using the expression from R_ACD. That gave:

|v|^2 = \frac{AC^2 \cdot AD^2}{4 k^2}. We have AC^2 = 2 r1 (h + r1) and AD^2 = 2 r2 (d + r2 - h). So:

|v|^2 = \frac{4 r1 r2 (h + r1)(d + r2 - h)}{4 k^2} = \frac{r1 r2 (h + r1)(d + r2 - h)}{k^2}. 

Now we need to simplify (h + r1)(d + r2 - h). We can compute that.

h + r1 = \frac{ (d + r1)^2 - r2^2 }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r1 - r2) S }{2d}. (We already have that.)
d + r2 - h = d + r2 - \frac{d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2}{2d} = \frac{2d^2 + 2d r2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)}{2d} = \frac{2d^2 + 2d r2 - d^2 - r1^2 + r2^2}{2d} = \frac{d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2}{2d}.

But we can also express d + r2 - h in terms of S and Δ2 maybe? Let's compute:

We have from NA^2: (h - d)^2 + k^2 = r2^2. But maybe we can find a similar factorization. Since h = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/(2d), then d + r2 - h = d + r2 - \frac{d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2}{2d} = \frac{2d^2 + 2d r2 - d^2 - r1^2 + r2^2}{2d} = \frac{d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2}{2d}. That's not obviously simplifying. But we can also compute (h + r1)(d + r2 - h) using the expressions we have. Perhaps we can find a nicer expression by noting that:

AC^2 = 2 r1 (h + r1) and AD^2 = 2 r2 (d + r2 - h). So the product is 4 r1 r2 (h + r1)(d + r2 - h). Then |v|^2 = (product)/(4 k^2). So that's fine. But we might want to compute |v|^2 explicitly in terms of d, r1, r2, k. But we have k^2 expressed. So we can compute:

|v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2}{k^2} * (h + r1)(d + r2 - h). So we need (h + r1)(d + r2 - h). Let's compute that.

First, compute (h + r1) = \frac{ (d + r1)^2 - r2^2 }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r1 - r2) S }{2d}. 
Now compute (d + r2 - h). We have:

d + r2 - h = d + r2 - \frac{d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2}{2d} = \frac{2d^2 + 2d r2 - d^2 - r1^2 + r2^2}{2d} = \frac{d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2}{2d}.

We can also try to express this as \frac{ (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 }{2d}? Let's compute (d + r2)^2 = d^2 + 2d r2 + r2^2. Then (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 = d^2 + 2d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2. That's exactly the numerator we have. So indeed:

d + r2 - h = \frac{ (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 }{2d}. But is that correct? Because we have d + r2 - h, not d + r2 + h. Let's compute:

(d + r2)^2 - r1^2 = (d^2 + 2d r2 + r2^2) - r1^2. That's not obviously equal to 2d (d + r2 - h). Let's compute 2d (d + r2 - h) = 2d^2 + 2d r2 - 2d h. And 2d h = d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2 (from h expression). So 2d^2 + 2d r2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) = d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2. That's the numerator we have. So indeed:

2d (d + r2 - h) = d^2 + 2d r2 - r1^2 + r2^2 = (d + r2)^2 - r1^2. So:

d + r2 - h = \frac{ (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 }{2d}. That's a valid expression. So we have:

(d + r2 - h) = \frac{ (d + r2)^2 - r1^2 }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r2 - r1)(d + r2 + r1) }{2d} = \frac{ (d + r2 - r1) S }{2d}. That's nice!

Thus:

h + r1 = \frac{ (d + r1 - r2) S }{2d}
d + r2 - h = \frac{ (d + r2 - r1) S }{2d}

Therefore:

(h + r1)(d + r2 - h) = \frac{ S^2 (d + r1 - r2)(d + r2 - r1) }{4 d^2} = \frac{ S^2 Δ2 }{4 d^2}.

Now then |v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2}{k^2} * \frac{ S^2 Δ2 }{4 d^2} = \frac{r1 r2 S^2 Δ2}{4 d^2 k^2}.

But also we have k^2 expressed in terms of Δ1 Δ2: k^2 = \frac{Δ1 Δ2}{4 d^2}. So substitute:

|v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2 S^2 Δ2}{4 d^2 * \frac{Δ1 Δ2}{4 d^2}} = \frac{r1 r2 S^2 Δ2}{4 d^2} * \frac{4 d^2}{Δ1 Δ2} = \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1}. 

Wait careful:

|v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2 S^2 Δ2}{4 d^2 k^2} = \frac{r1 r2 S^2 Δ2}{4 d^2 * \frac{Δ1 Δ2}{4 d^2}} = \frac{r1 r2 S^2 Δ2}{4 d^2} * \frac{4 d^2}{Δ1 Δ2} = \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1}.

Thus:

|v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1}, where Δ1 = (r1 + r2)^2 - d^2. That's a nice simplification! So |v|^2 is expressed without k. Good.

Now we also have a = r1 S / |v|^2, b = r2 S / |v|^2. So:

a = r1 S / \left( \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1} \right) = \frac{r1 S * Δ1}{r1 r2 S^2} = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S}.
Similarly, b = r2 S / \left( \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1} \right) = \frac{r2 S * Δ1}{r1 r2 S^2} = \frac{Δ1}{r1 S}.

Thus:

a = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S}
b = \frac{Δ1}{r1 S}.

This is nice. So the parameters are expressed in terms of Δ1, r1, r2, S. Note that Δ1 = (r1 + r2)^2 - d^2 > 0.

Now we also have v_x = \frac{ (r2 - r1) S }{2d}. So that's fine.

Now we need to compute O (circumcenter of BEF). We have u = E - B, w = F - B. Let's compute u and w explicitly using the new expressions.

Recall:

A = (h,k). B = (h,-k). v = (v_x, v_y). Then:

E = A + a v, F = A + b v.

Thus:

u = E - B = (A - B) + a v = (0, 2k) + a v.
w = F - B = (0, 2k) + b v.

Now we have a and b expressions. Also v components.

Now we want to solve for O:

u_x x_O + u_y y_O = 0
w_x x_O + w_y y_O = C2, where C2 = (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2.

We already have expressions for C2 in terms of b and |v|^2, but we can also compute C2 using the formulas we derived: C2 = \frac{r2 (r2 - r1) S^2}{2 |v|^2}. But we have |v|^2 expressed, so we can simplify C2. Let's compute:

|v|^2 = \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1}. Then:

C2 = \frac{r2 (r2 - r1) S^2}{2 * \frac{r1 r2 S^2}{Δ1}} = \frac{r2 (r2 - r1) S^2 * Δ1}{2 r1 r2 S^2} = \frac{ (r2 - r1) Δ1 }{2 r1}.

Thus:

C2 = \frac{ (r2 - r1) Δ1 }{2 r1}.

That's a nice expression. Note that we also have a = Δ1/(r2 S), b = Δ1/(r1 S). So a and b involve S, but C2 does not involve S. That's interesting.

Now we also have u_x = a v_x, u_y = 2k + a v_y. Similarly w_x = b v_x, w_y = 2k + b v_y.

We need to compute D = det(u,w) = u_x w_y - u_y w_x. We derived earlier that D = 2k v_x (u1 - u2) but with our definitions (u1 = a, u2 = b). Let's confirm with general notation:

Let α = a, β = b. Then u = (α v_x, 2k + α v_y), w = (β v_x, 2k + β v_y). Then det = α v_x * (2k + β v_y) - (2k + α v_y) * β v_x = 2k v_x (α - β). So D = 2k v_x (α - β). Since α = a, β = b. And α - β = a - b = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S} - \frac{Δ1}{r1 S} = \frac{Δ1}{S} ( \frac{1}{r2} - \frac{1}{r1} ) = \frac{Δ1}{S} \cdot \frac{r1 - r2}{r1 r2} = - \frac{Δ1 (r2 - r1)}{S r1 r2}. So α - β is negative, but that's okay.

Thus D = 2k v_x * (α - β) = 2k v_x * ( - \frac{Δ1 (r2 - r1)}{S r1 r2} ) = - \frac{2k v_x Δ1 (r2 - r1)}{S r1 r2}.

Now we have v_x expressed. v_x = \frac{ (r2 - r1) S }{2d}. So plug in:

D = - \frac{2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)}{S r1 r2} * \frac{ (r2 - r1) S }{2d} = - \frac{2k Δ1 (r2 - r1) * (r2 - r1) S}{S r1 r2 * 2d} = - \frac{2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)^2}{2d r1 r2} * (S cancels). So D = - \frac{k Δ1 (r2 - r1)^2}{d r1 r2}.

Thus D = - \frac{k Δ1 (r2 - r1)^2}{d r1 r2}. That's a scalar. Good.

Now we can compute O:

x_O = \frac{0 * w_y - u_y * C2}{D} = - \frac{u_y C2}{D}.
y_O = \frac{u_x * C2 - 0 * w_x}{D} = \frac{u_x C2}{D}.

Now we also have H. Let's compute H in terms of the coordinates. H = (x_P, y_H). We have x_P = \frac{d + r2 - r1}{2}. And y_H from earlier: y_H = \frac{ (x_P - m)(n - x_P) }{ y_P }? But here we have M = (0,0), N = (d,0), so m=0, n=d. Then the orthocenter H of triangle PMN is (x_P, \frac{ (x_P - 0)(d - x_P) }{ y_P } )? Actually we derived a general formula: H = (x_P, \frac{ (x_P - m)(n - x_P) }{ y_P } ) with m=0,n=d. So H_y = \frac{ x_P (d - x_P) }{ y_P }. Since y_P is negative. So H = (x_P, \frac{ x_P (d - x_P) }{ y_P } ). 

But note that earlier we had a different expression for H_y (with (x_P - m)(n - x_P) / y_P). That's correct. So H_y = \frac{ x_P (d - x_P) }{ y_P }. 

Now we also have v = (v_x, v_y). So we need to compute det(O - H, v) = (x_O - x_P) v_y - (y_O - y_H) v_x. This seems messy, but perhaps we can simplify by noting that O is determined by u and w, and we have expressions for u_x, u_y, w_x, w_y. Maybe we can find that O is collinear with something like (0,1) and (v_x, something), and H is something else. But I'm thinking that maybe there is a more clever analytic solution: Use coordinates where MN is the x-axis, and also place the line AP as something like the x-axis? But AP is not necessarily horizontal. But we can rotate such that AP becomes horizontal, then the line through H parallel to AP is horizontal. That would simplify L (horizontal line). But then the geometry becomes different because we need to maintain the circles' centers. Let's consider that.

We have two intersecting circles with centers M and N. The line MN is the line of centers. The common chord AB is perpendicular to MN. AP is some line through A and P (circumcenter of ACD). We could rotate the whole figure such that AP becomes horizontal, then L is horizontal. That might simplify the determinant condition because then the distance from O to L is just the vertical difference (if L is horizontal). But then we need to compute O and H in that rotated coordinate. However, the rotation is just a transformation, and the property we need to prove is invariant under rotations. So we can choose a coordinate system where AP is horizontal. That seems promising.

Let’s define a coordinate system where the line AP is horizontal (parallel to the x-axis). Then L, being parallel to AP, is also horizontal. So L is a horizontal line passing through H. Then the distance from O to L is simply |y_O - y_H|. The radius R is |O - B|. So we need to prove |y_O - y_H| = R. That seems maybe easier? But we also need to compute the coordinates of A, B, M, N, P, etc., in this coordinate system. However, we have the freedom to set up coordinates such that AP is horizontal. That's a rotation of the whole plane. That's allowed. So we can assume without loss of generality that AP is horizontal. Then we can place A and P conveniently.

But we also need to maintain that M and N are on the line MN, which is the line of centers. Since AP is horizontal, that doesn't conflict. However, we need to express the condition that A is an intersection of circles centered at M and N. That's a relation between coordinates. We can choose coordinates such that A = (0,0) or something? But we can also set A as origin. But then AP is horizontal, so P has some x-coordinate and y=0 (since AP horizontal). But we need to incorporate that P is circumcenter of ACD, and A, C, D are known points relative to M and N. That seems messy.

Alternatively, we can choose a coordinate system where MN is horizontal and AP is vertical? That would make L vertical. Then distance from O to L is horizontal difference. That's also simpler. But AP is not necessarily vertical. However, we can rotate such that AP becomes vertical, then L is vertical. That simplifies the distance because then the distance from O to L is just |x_O - x_H|. So we can set AP vertical. That might be easier. Let's try that.

Set AP vertical. Then L is vertical line through H. So L is x = x_H. Then the distance from O to L is |x_O - x_H|. The radius R is |O - B|. So we need to prove |x_O - x_H| = R. That seems perhaps simpler.

But we still need to compute coordinates of points relative to that orientation. However, we can choose a coordinate system where AP is vertical. That's a rotation. So we can assume without loss of generality that AP is vertical. Then we can set A and P on a vertical line. That's convenient.

Now we also have M and N on the line MN. Since AP is vertical, that's okay. But we need to incorporate the circles. Maybe we can place A at origin and AP vertical, so A = (0,0). Then P is on the vertical line (x=0) at some y-coordinate (since AP vertical). So P = (0, p) with p ≠ 0. Then L is the line through H parallel to AP (vertical), so L is vertical line x = x_H. So L is x = x_H. That's a vertical line. The distance from O to L is |x_O - x_H|. That's simple. So we need to show that |x_O - x_H| = R. So we need to compute x_O and x_H. That seems plausible.

Now we need to express everything in coordinates with A = (0,0). But then A is a point on both circles Ω and Γ. That's fine. Then B is the reflection of A across MN (since MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB). But we don't know MN's orientation. However, we have freedom to set up coordinate system such that AP is vertical. That's a rotation. Then we also have the line MN (centers line). But we can also translate such that A is at origin. That's a translation. So we can assume A = (0,0). Then AP is vertical, so P lies somewhere on the y-axis (x=0). Then the line AP is the y-axis. So L is vertical line through H (since parallel to AP). So L is x = x_H. So we need to compute x_H. H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. M and N are points on the line MN. Since AP is vertical, that doesn't fix MN orientation. However, we also know that A is an intersection of circles with centers M and N. So M and N are points such that MA = r1, NA = r2. With A at origin, that gives |M| = r1, |N| = r2. Also, the line MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB. Since A is at origin, B is some point such that the midpoint of AB lies on MN. That's a bit messy.

But maybe we can use the coordinate system where M = (0,0) and N = (d,0) as before, and then rotate such that AP becomes vertical. That rotation would change the coordinates of A, B, etc. But then we can compute expressions. However, we already have a nice expression for |v|^2 and a,b, etc., in the M=(0,0) coordinate. That coordinate seems to have given simplifications. Perhaps we can continue with that coordinate and then compute O and H, and then the determinant. I'm trying to see if the determinant simplifies nicely.

Let's compute O in that coordinate, and also H, and then compute det(O - H, v). We have expressions for u, w, D, C2, and also v_x, v_y. We'll need to compute x_O and y_O in terms of k and v_x, v_y, etc. Then compute O - H. The vector v is known. Then det. That seems heavy but maybe many terms cancel.

Let's try to compute O explicitly using the formulas we derived:

x_O = - (u_y C2) / D
y_O = (u_x C2) / D

where u = (α v_x, 2k + α v_y), with α = a. And D = 2k v_x (α - β) with β = b. So:

x_O = - ( (2k + α v_y) C2 ) / ( 2k v_x (α - β) )
y_O = ( α v_x C2 ) / ( 2k v_x (α - β) ) = ( α C2 ) / ( 2k (α - β) ).

Thus y_O simplifies nicely: y_O = \frac{α C2}{2k (α - β)}.

Now α - β = a - b = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S} - \frac{Δ1}{r1 S} = \frac{Δ1}{S} \cdot \frac{r1 - r2}{r1 r2} = - \frac{Δ1 (r2 - r1)}{S r1 r2}.

So:

α - β = - \frac{Δ1 (r2 - r1)}{S r1 r2}.

Then:

y_O = \frac{α C2}{2k} * \frac{1}{α - β} = \frac{α C2}{2k} * \left( - \frac{S r1 r2}{Δ1 (r2 - r1)} \right) = - \frac{α C2 S r1 r2}{2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)}.

Now α = a = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S}. So α C2 = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S} * C2. Then:

y_O = - \frac{ (Δ1 C2)/(r2 S) * S r1 r2 }{2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)} = - \frac{ (Δ1 C2) * S r1 r2 }{ r2 S * 2k Δ1 (r2 - r1)}? Wait careful:

α C2 = \frac{Δ1}{r2 S} * C2. That means that MN is the set of points equidistant from A and B. If MN is vertical, then AB is horizontal. The midpoint of AB lies on MN. So if we set MN as the vertical line x = 0, then the midpoint of AB is on that line. But A and B are not necessarily on that line. So we can choose coordinates such that MN is the vertical line x = m (some constant). Then AB is horizontal. The midpoint of AB lies on MN. So we can set up: Let MN be the vertical line x = c. Then AB is horizontal. The midpoint of AB is (c, y0) for some y0. Then A and B are symmetric across that vertical line. So A = (c - a, y0), B = (c + a, y0) for some a>0. So that's a good coordinate choice. That's similar to before but with MN vertical. So we can adopt:

- MN is vertical line x = m (some constant). Then AB is horizontal.
- A and B have same y-coordinate, say y = y0. Their x-coordinates are symmetric about x = m.
- The centers M and N lie on MN, so they have x-coordinate m. Their y-coordinates are something.

But then AP is some line through A and P. P is circumcenter of ACD. That seems messy.

However, the earlier coordinate with M=(0,0), N=(d,0) (horizontal MN) gave nice expressions for |v|^2, a, b, and y_O = -Δ1/(4k). That seems promising. The y_O expression is simple. The H_y expression we derived as - d k / S. So y_O - y_H = -Δ1/(4k) + (d k)/S.

Now note that the radius R = |O - B|. Since O_y = -Δ1/(4k). B = (h,-k). So O - B = (x_O - h, -Δ1/(4k) + k). The squared distance is (x_O - h)^2 + (k - Δ1/(4k))^2. That's messy. But maybe we can also compute O_x - x_H and then the determinant with v. However, we might be able to prove tangency by showing that the power of H with respect to (BEF) is equal to the square of the distance from H to the line L (which is zero), but that doesn't give tangency. Actually we need L to be tangent, which is equivalent to that H is the pole of L with respect to the circle. But we could compute the polar of H with respect to circle (BEF). That polar is a line. If we can show that this polar is L, then since H is outside the circle, the polar is not tangent. But we need L to be tangent. So not that.

Maybe we can find a relationship: The line L is the radical axis of two circles, one of which is (BEF). If we can show that L is the radical axis of (BEF) and the circle with diameter MN, then the distance from O to L equals something like (R^2 - something). Not.

I think I need to reconsider the approach. Perhaps there is a more elegant synthetic solution using inversion with respect to the circle with diameter MN, or with respect to the circle centered at A. Let's try to see what happens under inversion.

We have circles Ω and Γ intersecting at A and B. Consider the inversion with center A. Under this inversion, the circles Ω and Γ become lines (since they pass through A). Because a circle through the center of inversion becomes a line not passing through the center. So after inversion about A, Ω becomes a line L_Ω (not passing through A), Γ becomes a line L_Γ. The points C and D (on Ω and Γ) become points C' and D' on those lines. The line MN (centers line) becomes some circle through A (since it's a line not passing through A? Actually MN is a line that does not pass through A generally, because A is off that line (except when A is directly above M or N). So under inversion, MN becomes a circle passing through A. That might complicate.

The circles Ω (centered at M) and Γ (centered at N) are orthogonal to the circle with diameter MN? Ω has center M, radius r1. The distance between M and O_mid is d/2. So the condition for orthogonality is (d/2)^2 = r1^2 + (d/2)^2? That would imply r1=0. So not orthogonal. So not that.

Maybe consider the circle with center at the midpoint of AB and radius k. That's the circle with diameter AB? Actually the circle with diameter AB has center at midpoint of AB and radius k. That circle is orthogonal to the circle with center at the midpoint of MN? Not sure.

Let's try to find a synthetic approach by analyzing angles.

We have H (orthocenter of PMN). Since PMN is a triangle, H is the intersection of altitudes. The altitude from P is perpendicular to MN. The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. So H is the orthocenter.

Now AP is a line. The line through H parallel to AP is L. So L is a line such that it is parallel to AP, and it passes through H. So L is the translate of AP by vector H - A.

Now consider the circle BEF. Since E and F are on AP, and B is the reflection of A across MN, perhaps the circle BEF is symmetric to the circle passing through A and the reflections of E and F across MN. But maybe we can prove that L is the radical axis of circles (BEF) and (A? )? Hmm.

Let's try to compute det(O - H, v) using the linear system approach, but also using the expressions for u, w, D, etc. We have O expressed in terms of u and w. But we also have H expressed. ## Solution
We present a solution using analytic geometry.  After a suitable rigid motion we place the figure in a coordinate system that simplifies the computations.

### 1.  Choice of coordinates
Let the line of centers \(MN\) be the \(x\)-axis.  Denote  

\[
M=(0,0),\qquad N=(d,0),\qquad d=MN>0.
\]

Because the two circles intersect, we have \(r_1<r_2\) and  

\[
(r_2-r_1)^2<d^2<(r_1+r_2)^2.
\]

Let \(A\) and \(B\) be the two intersection points.  The line \(MN\) is the perpendicular bisector of \(AB\); therefore \(A\) and \(B\) are symmetric with respect to the \(x\)-axis.  We may write  

\[
A=(h,k),\qquad B=(h,-k),\qquad k>0.
\]

The distances give  

\[
h^2+k^2=r_1^2,\qquad (h-d)^2+k^2=r_2^2.
\]

Solving for \(h\) we obtain  

\[
h=\frac{d^2+r_1^2-r_2^2}{2d}.
\tag{1}
\]

### 2.  The point \(P\) (circumcenter of \(\triangle ACD\))
The points \(C\) and \(D\) lie on the line \(MN\) (the \(x\)-axis) and satisfy  

\[
C=(-r_1,0),\qquad D=(d+r_2,0)
\]

(the order \(C\!-\!M\!-\!N\!-\!D\) forces the signs).  

Because \(C\) and \(D\) are on the \(x\)-axis, the perpendicular bisector of \(CD\) is vertical; its equation is  

\[
x_P=\frac{-r_1+(d+r_2)}{2}=\frac{d+r_2-r_1}{2}.
\tag{2}
\]

Now \(P\) must also be equidistant from \(A\) and \(C\).  Equating \(|PA|^2=|PC|^2\) yields  

\[
(y_P-k)^2 + (x_P-h)^2 = y_P^2 + (x_P+r_1)^2.
\]

After cancellation we obtain  

\[
y_P = -\frac{x_P\,(r_1+h)}{k}.
\tag{3}
\]

Thus  

\[
P=\Bigl(\frac{d+r_2-r_1}{2},\;-\frac{x_P\,(r_1+h)}{k}\Bigr).
\]

### 3.  The direction vector \(v = \overrightarrow{AP}\)
\[
v=(v_x,v_y)=(x_P-h,\;y_P-k).
\]

Using the expressions of \(h\) and \(x_P\) we compute  

\[
v_x = \frac{(r_2-r_1)\,S}{2d},\qquad 
S=d+r_1+r_2.
\tag{4}
\]

For \(v_y\) we use (3) and \(y_P-k\).  A convenient simplification is obtained by first noting  

\[
r_1+h = \frac{(d+r_1)^2-r_2^2}{2d}= \frac{(d+r_1-r_2)S}{2d},
\]

\[
x_P\,(r_1+h)=\frac{S\,(d+r_2-r_1)(d+r_1-r_2)}{4d}= \frac{S\,\Delta_2}{4d},
\]

where  

\[
\Delta_2 = d^2-(r_2-r_1)^2>0.
\]

Hence  

\[
y_P = -\frac{S\,\Delta_2}{4d\,k}.
\tag{5}
\]

Consequently  

\[
v_y = y_P-k = -k-\frac{S\,\Delta_2}{4d\,k}.
\tag{6}
\]

### 4.  The points \(E\) and \(F\) (second intersections of \(AP\) with \(\Omega\) and \(\Gamma\))
The line \(AP\) is parametrized by  

\[
X = A + t\,v,\qquad t\in\mathbb R.
\]

The intersection with \(\Omega\) (center \(M\), radius \(r_1\)) gives  

\[
|A + t v - M|^2 = r_1^2.
\]

Because \(A\) is on \(\Omega\), we have \(|A-M|^2=r_1^2\).  Expanding and simplifying yields  

\[
t\,(2A\!\cdot\!v + t\,|v|^2)=0,
\]

so besides \(t=0\) (point \(A\)) we get  

\[
t_E = -\frac{2\,A\!\cdot\!v}{|v|^2}.
\]

Similarly, for \(\Gamma\) (center \(N\), radius \(r_2\)) we obtain  

\[
t_F = -\frac{2\,(A-N)\!\cdot\!v}{|v|^2}.
\]

Now  

\[
A\!\cdot\!v = h\,v_x + k\,v_y = h\,v_x + k\,(y_P-k).
\]

But a direct computation using the expressions (3) and (4) gives a simpler relation.  Instead we compute the dot products with the help of the circumradius of \(\triangle ACD\).  

The triangle \(ACD\) has side lengths  

\[
AC^2 = 2r_1(h+r_1),\qquad AD^2 = 2r_2(d+r_2-h),\qquad CD = d+r_1+r_2 = S.
\]

Its circumradius is  

\[
R_{ACD} = \frac{AC\cdot AD}{2k}.
\]

Hence  

\[
|v|^2 = |P-A|^2 = R_{ACD}^2 = \frac{AC^2\cdot AD^2}{4k^2}
        = \frac{r_1 r_2\,S^2}{\Delta_1},
\tag{7}
\]

where  

\[
\Delta_1 = (r_1+r_2)^2-d^2 > 0.
\]

Now  

\[
A\!\cdot\!v = -\frac{r_1 S}{2},
\qquad
(A-N)\!\cdot\!v = -\frac{r_2 S}{2}.
\]

(This follows from the equalities \(|PA|^2=|PC|^2\) and \(|PA|^2=|PD|^2\) after algebraic manipulation; a quick verification is given in the remark at the end of the solution.)  

Therefore  

\[
t_E = -\frac{2\,(-\frac{r_1 S}{2})}{|v|^2}= \frac{r_1 S}{|v|^2},\qquad
t_F = -\frac{2\,(-\frac{r_2 S}{2})}{|v|^2}= \frac{r_2 S}{|v|^2}.
\]

Thus  

\[
E = A + \frac{r_1 S}{|v|^2}\,v,\qquad 
F = A + \frac{r_2 S}{|v|^2}\,v.
\tag{8}
\]

### 5.  The orthocentre \(H\) of \(\triangle PMN\)

With \(M=(0,0)\) and \(N=(d,0)\), the altitude from \(P\) is vertical; therefore \(H\) has the same \(x\)-coordinate as \(P\):

\[
x_H = x_P = \frac{d+r_2-r_1}{2}.
\]

The altitude from \(M\) is perpendicular to \(PN\).  The vector \(PN = (d-x_P,\,-y_P)\); a direction vector perpendicular to \(PN\) is \((y_P,\,d-x_P)\).  The line through \(M\) with this direction is  

\[
(0,0)+\lambda\,(y_P,\,d-x_P).
\]

Intersecting with \(x=x_P\) gives \(\lambda = \dfrac{x_P}{y_P}\).  Hence  

\[
y_H = \lambda\,(d-x_P) = \frac{x_P\,(d-x_P)}{y_P}.
\tag{9}
\]

Substituting the expressions for \(x_P\), \(d-x_P\) and \(y_P\) (5) we obtain after simplification  

\[
y_H = -\frac{d\,k}{S}.
\tag{10}
\]

(Indeed, \(x_P(d-x_P) = \frac{(d+r_2-r_1)(d+r_1-r_2)}{4}= \frac{\Delta_2}{4}\) and \(y_P = -\frac{S\Delta_2}{4dk}\); thus \(y_H = \frac{\Delta_2/4}{-S\Delta_2/(4dk)} = -\frac{dk}{S}\).)

Therefore  

\[
H=\Bigl(\frac{d+r_2-r_1}{2},\;-\frac{d\,k}{S}\Bigr).
\tag{11}
\]

### 6.  The line \(L\) through \(H\) parallel to \(AP\)

The direction of \(AP\) is \(v\); hence \(L\) is the set of points  

\[
X = H + s\,v,\qquad s\in\mathbb R.
\tag{12}
\]

### 7.  The circumcircle of \(\triangle BEF\)

We denote by \(\omega\) the circumcircle of \(BEF\).  Let \(O\) be its centre and \(R\) its radius.

First compute the vectors  

\[
u = E - B,\qquad w = F - B.
\]

Since \(B=(h,-k)\) and \(A=(h,k)\), we have  

\[
A-B = (0,\,2k).
\]

Thus  

\[
u = (0,\,2k) + \frac{r_1 S}{|v|^2}\,v,\qquad
w = (0,\,2k) + \frac{r_2 S}{|v|^2}\,v.
\]

Now \(u\) and \(w\) are linearly independent because \(r_1\neq r_2\) and \(v\) is not parallel to \((0,2k)\) (otherwise \(AP\) would be vertical, but \(v\) has a non‑zero \(x\)-component by (4) because \(r_2>r_1\) and \(S>0\)).  

The centre \(O\) satisfies  

\[
|O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 \;\Longrightarrow\; 2\,O\!\cdot\!(E-B) = |E|^2-|B|^2,
\]  

\[
|O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2 \;\Longrightarrow\; 2\,O\!\cdot\!(F-B) = |F|^2-|B|^2.
\]

Because \(|E|^2-|B|^2 = 0\) (as shown below), the first equation becomes  

\[
O\!\cdot\!u = 0.
\tag{13}
\]

For the second equation we compute  

\[
|F|^2-|B|^2 = 2\,\frac{r_2 S}{|v|^2}\,(A\!\cdot\!v) + \frac{r_2^2 S^2}{|v|^4}\,|v|^2
           = -\frac{r_1 r_2 S^2}{|v|^2} + \frac{r_2^2 S^2}{|v|^2}
           = \frac{r_2\,(r_2-r_1)\,S^2}{|v|^2}.
\]

Using \(|v|^2 = \dfrac{r_1 r_2 S^2}{\Delta_1}\) from (7) we obtain  

\[
|F|^2-|B|^2 = \frac{(r_2-r_1)\,\Delta_1}{2\,r_1}.
\]

Therefore  

\[
O\!\cdot\!w = \frac{(r_2-r_1)\,\Delta_1}{4\,r_1}.
\tag{14}
\]

Now we solve the linear system (13)–(14) for \(O\).  Write \(O=(x,y)\).  Then  

\[
\begin{cases}
u_x\,x + u_y\,y = 0,\\[4pt]
w_x\,x + w_y\,y = C_2,
\end{cases}
\qquad 
C_2 = \frac{(r_2-r_1)\,\Delta_1}{4\,r_1}.
\]

The determinant is  

\[
D = u_x w_y - u_y w_x.
\]

Compute  

\[
u_x = \frac{r_1 S}{|v|^2}\,v_x,\qquad u_y = 2k + \frac{r_1 S}{|v|^2}\,v_y,
\]  

\[
w_x = \frac{r_2 S}{|v|^2}\,v_x,\qquad w_y = 2k + \frac{r_2 S}{|v|^2}\,v_y.
\]

Using the expression for \(v_x\) and \(v_y\) we find after a straightforward simplification  

\[
D = \frac{2k\,v_x\,(r_1-r_2)}{|v|^2}
    = -\frac{k\,(r_2-r_1)^2\,\Delta_1}{d\,r_1\,r_2}.
\tag{15}
\]

(Indeed, \(v_x = \dfrac{(r_2-r_1)S}{2d}\) and \(\dfrac{r_1-r_2}{|v|^2} = \dfrac{r_1-r_2}{r_1 r_2 S^2/\Delta_1} = \dfrac{\Delta_1\,(r_1-r_2)}{r_1 r_2 S^2}\); then \(D = 2k\,v_x\,\dfrac{r_1-r_2}{|v|^2} = 2k\,\dfrac{(r_2-r_1)S}{2d}\,\dfrac{\Delta_1\,(r_1-r_2)}{r_1 r_2 S^2} = -\dfrac{k\,(r_2-r_1)^2\,\Delta_1}{d\,r_1 r_2}\).)

Now solve for \(x\) and \(y\):

\[
x = \frac{0\cdot w_y - u_y C_2}{D} = -\frac{u_y C_2}{D},
\qquad
y = \frac{u_x C_2 - 0\cdot w_x}{D} = \frac{u_x C_2}{D}.
\]

Plugging the values of \(u_x\), \(u_y\) and \(C_2\) we simplify the expressions.

First compute \(y\):

\[
y = \frac{u_x C_2}{D}
   = \frac{\dfrac{r_1 S}{|v|^2}\,v_x \cdot C_2}{D}
   = \frac{\dfrac{r_1 S}{|v|^2}\,v_x \cdot \dfrac{(r_2-r_1)\Delta_1}{4r_1}}{D}
   = \frac{S\,v_x\,\Delta_1}{4|v|^2\,} \cdot \frac{1}{D}.
\]

But \(D\) is given by (15), and \(v_x\) is known.  A direct simplification yields  

\[
y = -\frac{\Delta_1}{4k}.
\tag{16}
\]

(One can verify: \(v_x = \dfrac{(r_2-r_1)S}{2d}\), \(|v|^2 = \dfrac{r_1 r_2 S^2}{\Delta_1}\), so  

\[
\frac{S\,v_x\,\Delta_1}{4|v|^2} = \frac{S\cdot\frac{(r_2-r_1)S}{2d}\cdot\Delta_1}{4\cdot\frac{r_1 r_2 S^2}{\Delta_1}} = \frac{(r_2-r_1)S^2\Delta_1}{2d} \cdot \frac{\Delta_1}{4 r_1 r_2 S^2} = \frac{(r_2-r_1)\Delta_1^2}{8 d\, r_1 r_2}.
\]

Then \(y = \dfrac{(r_2-r_1)\Delta_1^2}{8 d\, r_1 r_2} \cdot \dfrac{1}{D}\).  Substitute \(D = -\dfrac{k\,(r_2-r_1)^2\,\Delta_1}{d\,r_1 r_2}\):

\[
y = \frac{(r_2-r_1)\Delta_1^2}{8 d\, r_1 r_2} \cdot \frac{1}{-\dfrac{k\,(r_2-r_1)^2\,\Delta_1}{d\,r_1 r_2}}
   = -\frac{\Delta_1}{4k}.
\]

So (16) is correct.)

Now compute \(x\):

\[
x = -\frac{u_y C_2}{D}
   = -\frac{(2k + \frac{r_1 S}{|v|^2}\,v_y)\cdot \dfrac{(r_2-r_1)\Delta_1}{4r_1}}{D}.
\]

Substituting the expression for \(D\) and simplifying (this is somewhat lengthy but straightforward) we obtain  

\[
x = \frac{d\,r_2\,k}{S\,(r_2-r_1)} - \frac{d\,r_1\,k}{S\,(r_2-r_1)}? \text{ Actually we will not need the explicit value of }x.
\]

However, we will need the determinant \(\det(O-H,v)\).  Let's compute \(O-H\) in terms of known quantities.  From (16) we have  

\[
O_y = -\frac{\Delta_1}{4k}.
\]

From (11) we have  

\[
H_y = -\frac{d\,k}{S}.
\]

Thus  

\[
O_y - H_y = -\frac{\Delta_1}{4k} + \frac{d\,k}{S}.
\tag{17}
\]

Now the distance from \(O\) to the line \(L\) is  

\[
\operatorname{dist}(O,L)=\frac{|\det(O-H,v)|}{|v|}.
\]

We need to show that this distance equals \(R = |O-B|\).  Because the algebra is manageable, we will compute \(\det(O-H,v)\) and \(R^2|v|^2\) and prove equality.

### 8.  Computing \(\det(O-H,v)\)

Let \(v=(v_x,v_y)\).  The determinant is  

\[
\det(O-H,v) = (x_O - x_P)\,v_y - (y_O - y_H)\,v_x.
\]

We have \(x_P\) and \(y_H\) from (2) and (10).  Also we have \(y_O\) from (16).  The term \(x_O\) is still unknown, but we can eliminate it by using the fact that \(O\) lies on the perpendicular bisector of \(EF\).  That condition gives a linear equation that relates \(x_O\) and \(y_O\).  Alternatively, we can solve for \(x_O\) directly from the linear system (13)–(14) and then compute the determinant.  We already have an expression for \(y_O\); we can compute \(x_O\) from the formula \(x_O = -\dfrac{u_y C_2}{D}\) after substituting \(u_y\) and \(D\).  However, we can also compute the determinant using the vector forms and the known expressions for \(u\) and \(w\).  Let's attempt a more systematic approach.

Recall the linear system for \(O\):

\[
u_x x + u_y y = 0, \qquad w_x x + w_y y = C_2.
\]

We solved for \(y\) and obtained \(y = -\dfrac{\Delta_1}{4k}\).  For \(x\) we have  

\[
x = -\frac{u_y C_2}{D}.
\]

Now \(u_y = 2k + \dfrac{r_1 S}{|v|^2}\,v_y\).  Substitute \(v_y\) from (6) and simplify.  This will give \(x\) in a somewhat messy form.  But perhaps we can avoid explicit \(x\) by noting that  

\[
\det(O-H,v) = \det(O-H,v) = \det( (x_O - x_P,\; y_O - y_H),\; (v_x, v_y) ).
\]

We can also compute the determinant as  

\[
\det(O-H,v) = \det(O-H,v) = \det( (x_O - x_P,\; y_O - y_H),\; (v_x, v_y) ) = \det( (x_O - x_P,\; y_O - y_H),\; v ).
\]

Now maybe we can find that \(x_O - x_P\) is proportional to something like \(k\) or \(v_x\) and then the determinant simplifies.  Let's compute \(x_O\) using the formula and then find \(x_O - x_P\).  We'll need to simplify \(u_y C_2 / D\).  We have \(D = -\dfrac{k\,(r_2-r_1)^2\,\Delta_1}{d\,r_1 r_2}\).  Also \(C_2 = \dfrac{(r_2-r_1)\Delta_1}{4 r_1}\).  So  

\[
\frac{C_2}{D} = \frac{\dfrac{(r_2-r_1)\Delta_1}{4 r_1}}{-\dfrac{k\,(r_2-r_1)^2\,\Delta_1}{d\,r_1 r_2}} = -\frac{d\,r_2}{4 k\,(r_2-r_1)}.
\]

Therefore  

\[
x = -\frac{u_y C_2}{D} = -\frac{u_y}{D} \cdot C_2? Actually we have x = -\frac{u_y C_2}{D} = -u_y \cdot \frac{C_2}{D} = -u_y \cdot \left( -\frac{d\,r_2}{4 k\,(r_2-r_1)} \right) = \frac{u_y\,d\,r_2}{4 k\,(r_2-r_1)}.
\]

Now \(u_y = 2k + \dfrac{r_1 S}{|v|^2}\,v_y\).  Plug \(v_y\) from (6).  So  

\[
u_y = 2k + \frac{r_1 S}{|v|^2}\left( -k - \frac{S\,\Delta_2}{4d\,k} \right) = 2k - \frac{r_1 S\,k}{|v|^2} - \frac{r_1 S^2\,\Delta_2}{4d\,k\,|v|^2}.
\]

Now \(|v|^2 = \dfrac{r_1 r_2 S^2}{\Delta_1}\), so  

\[
\frac{r_1 S}{|v|^2} = \frac{r_1 S}{\dfrac{r_1 r_2 S^2}{\Delta_1}} = \frac{\Delta_1
